# Developer Console

The **Developer Console** is the platform used to create both Ontology SDK applications as well as OAuth clients (formerly known as third-party applications in Control Panel).

To request access to Developer Console, follow the steps in [Application Access](foundry/beta/beta-applications/).

>**Important Note**<br> Getting access to Developer Console only lets you view/manage Third  Party Applications(TPAs) or OAuth clients created for you. For creation please raise a request as follows.

## Third Party Applications (TPA)

Third-party applications are external software applications that can connect to Foundry to access data, perform actions, or integrate with Foundry's capabilities. They use the OAuth 2.0 framework for secure authentication and authorization.

There are two options when it comes to authorization:

1. **Client credentials grant:** When you want to use a service user type of workflow wherein the actions made by the TPA would not be associated with any Foundry user. Example: If you are calling Foundry APIs from Code Repository and would like to use a service user like user instead of personal token from a developer.
1. **Authorization code grant:** When you are building an interactive user facing workflow and would like the TPA to act on behalf of the user with their permissions(either entire or with limited scope). Example - App Service applications

To learn more about the types and implementation workflow, you can refer to the documentation of [Writing OAuth2 clients for Foundry](https://palantir.mcloud.merckgroup.com/docs/foundry/platform-security-third-party/writing-oauth2-clients/).

## Application scopes

1. **Unscoped:** The application will have access to all operations and resources depending on the authorizing user's permissions. This is however not necessary in majority of the cases and best practice is to limit the operations and resources necessary for the requirement in hand.
2. **Scoped:** The application will only have access to a subset of operations and resources, further restricted by the authorizing user's permissions i.e if a user has access to project A and project B, and only project B is added in the Platform SDK section, then the user can only access project B from the application. These can be defined as below -

#### Platform SDK
Under Platform SDK, you can define the projects that the application can access and the allowed operations. The projects can be added by clicking on the "Add project" button in the Project access section and allowed operations can be configured under the Client-allowed operations section

<img src="/assets/foundry/platform-sdk.svg">

#### Ontology SDK
Under Ontology SDK, you can define the Object types, Action types, Interfaces and Functions that the application can access.

Take for example, if you want to call the action to create a Use Case access request [ARO - Create (UseCase)](https://palantir.mcloud.merckgroup.com/workspace/ontology/action-type/ri.actions.main.action-type.756d7605-5485-40e8-a823-7d7b081dd522/parameters). In this case, the resources you will need to add are -

*Object Types*
- Use Case
- [MDAO] Entitlement(If Access Request via Entitlement is needed)
- Permission Preset (If Access Request via Preset is needed)
- Foundry Project
- Foundry Group (If Access Request is not via Preset)

*Link Types needed*
- Use Case <-> Permission Preset(If Access Request via Preset is needed)
- Use Case <-> Foundry Project
- Foundry Project <-> Foundry Group (Link type - Compliant Foundry Editor or Viewer Groups or Subgroups) (If Access Request is not via Preset)

*Actions needed*
- ARO - Create (UseCase)

Below images display, how resources can be added and to check if the necessary link types are included

<img src="/assets/foundry/ontology-sdk-1.svg">
<br>Select the Merck Ontology<br>

<img src="/assets/foundry/ontology-sdk-2.svg">
<br>Add an Object under Object types<br>

<img src="/assets/foundry/ontology-sdk-3.svg">
<br>View all the Link types for an Object type<br>

<img src="/assets/foundry/ontology-sdk-4.svg">
<br>Include specific Link types<br>

<img src="/assets/foundry/ontology-sdk-5.svg">
<br>Verify all the necessary link types are included<br>

<img src="/assets/foundry/ontology-sdk-6.svg">
<br>AIP Logic and AIP Agent Studio published functions can be added under Functions section<br>

<br>**Note:** When adding Ontology resources, other resources like Link types between added Object types are automatically included. In case it is not, you can add them manually as shown in above images.<br>

## How to request a TPA?

To request a TPA, you will need to raise a [Foundry Issue](https://palantir.mcloud.merckgroup.com/workspace/issues-app/) with following details

1. The Use Case this request is for.
2. Description of why the TPA is needed and planned implementation approach.
3. Will Ontology SDK be used?


4. If the authorization is of **client credentials type** and Ontology SDK isn't used, you need to also include 

- Who should be the owner/delegates? - These are the users that will recieve the client id and client secret
- Should the TPA have access to Use Case projects and if so also specify the role it needs i.e. viewer or editor. If the request is approved and TPA is created, the automation in place creates an Access Request Object(ARO) to the requested Use Case project and a Use Case owner will have to approve it to for the TPA to get access.


5. Application Scope needed i.e. Scoped or Unscoped.

The platform team will add the respective sector admin(s) to review the request. For client credentials grant type without OSDK usage, the sector admins can create it for you. For authorization code grant type TPAs, the platform team will be creating the TPA and granting you editor role on the application.

You can also refer to this Wiki page from ACE [OAuth 2.0 - Delegates User Permission to access UPTIMIZE Foundry resources](https://wiki-nregprod.merckgroup.com/spaces/AKB/pages/637345816/OAuth+2.0+-+Delegates+User+Permission+to+access+UPTIMIZE+Foundry+resources) on this topic where an example has also been provided along with helpful links.

## Website Hosting


> **Prequisites**:<br> 1. Have access to Developer Console<br>2. Have a purely Client-facing application (authorization code grant alone enabled) TPA created.



Once you have the pre-requisites taken care of, you can either use the same issue as the one you raised for creation of the TPA or create a new one with a link to your website domain request. Your requested domain name should follow the naming convention defined below.

### Website naming convention
**Format**: 
```<sector-short>[-<ou>|-uc-<uc-name>]-<app-name>.palantir.mcloud.merckgroup.com```

* **Sector (required)**: ```hc | el | ls | mgf | global (lowercase)```
* **OU (Organizational Unit, optional)**: short, lowercase, kebab-case (use when it materially disambiguates ownership)
* **UC (Use Case, optional)**: use-case scope for temporary/experimental sites: uc-<uc-name> (lower-kebab-case)
* **App name (required)**: lower-kebab-case, concise, descriptive

**Examples**:
* Sector + OU + app: ```el-boost-material-portal.palantir.mcloud.merckgroup.com```
* Sector + app (no OU): ```hc-clinical-insights.palantir.mcloud.merckgroup.com```
* Use case scoped: ```ls-uc-myanalysis-awesome-app-dev.palantir.mcloud.merckgroup.com```
* Global/shared: ```global-data-catalog.palantir.mcloud.merckgroup.com```


> **Important Note**<br> Users who can visit your website can be defined under "Sharing and tokens" -> "Login to hosted website". By default, the platform team will add the Use Case project viewer and editor groups. If you would like to restrict it to limited set of users or enable it to a wider group please mention this.


### Next Steps
1. The Redirect URLs for your website can be configured under OAuth and scopes and these will need to be added to CORS settings on the stack. The below urls are already added -
    - https://arbor-containers.palantirfoundry.com - Palantir domain where VS Code workspace is served from
    - http://localhost:8080 - For local development
    - All foundry hosted website domains

In case of any other redirect url for your website, raise an issue to request addition of it in CORS setting as these need to be reviewed by platform admins.

2. If you would also like to embed this website within Foundry like in a workshop application, you need to raise an issue so that it can be added under the Content Security Policy(CSP). Since wilcards are not supported in case of CSP, the platform team will need to manually add each hosted website that is requested.

# Email Service

The UPTIMIZE Email Service allows you to send branded, UPTIMIZE-themed emails from within Foundry through Python Transforms or Action Types.

Browse the following pages to learn about the Email Service:

---

# UPTIMIZE Internal Notification Service

## What is the Notification Service?

The UPTIMIZE Internal Notification Service lets you **send emails directly from Foundry** -- for example, to notify users when a pipeline finishes, to alert a team about a data quality issue, or to send periodic reports.

All emails are automatically styled with the official **UPTIMIZE email template**, so they look professional and consistent. You do not need to worry about email formatting or layout -- just provide the subject, content, and recipients.

### What can I do with it?

Here are some common use cases:

- **Automated alerts:** Notify a team when a data pipeline completes or when a data quality check fails.
- **User-triggered notifications:** Send a confirmation email when someone submits a request through a Workshop application.
- **Periodic reporting:** Email a summary or report to a group of stakeholders on a regular schedule.
- **Workflow notifications:** Inform users when an approval is needed, a task is assigned, or a status changes.

### How does the email look?

Every email sent through this service uses the UPTIMIZE-branded template. The email includes:

- A **sender name** (the `topic` you specify -- for example, "Data Quality Team" or "Pipeline Monitor"), shown as the sender in Outlook.
- A **subject line** you define.
- An **email body** that supports full HTML -- you can include formatted text, links, tables, images, and more.
- The standard UPTIMIZE branding and footer.

### Key capabilities

- **Flexible recipients:** Send to plain email addresses, Foundry User IDs, or Foundry Group IDs (the service resolves IDs to email addresses automatically when using the Python library).
- **Full TO / CC / BCC support:** Just like a regular email.
- **HTML content:** Style your email body with links, bold text, lists, tables, and more.
- **Auditability:** Every email sent through the service is logged, so you can track what was sent and when.

**Webinar:** For a visual walkthrough, watch the recording by Simon Heckmann: [Episode II: Email Notifications with the Notification Library](https://mdigital.sharepoint.com/sites/Stream-SimonHeckmann/_layouts/15/stream.aspx?id=%2Fsites%2FStream-SimonHeckmann%2FShared+Documents%2FStream+Migrated+Videos%2FUPTIMIZE+Webinars%2FEpisode+II_+Email+Notifications+with+the+Notification+Library-20221101_090927.mp4&referrer=StreamWebApp.Web&referrerScenario=AddressBarCopied.view.7c149692-0d9f-492b-9b25-96c27d6bcbb2)

---

## Getting Access

Before you can use the Notification Service, you need to request access. This is a one-time step.

1. Open the [User Access Management Application](https://palantir.mcloud.merckgroup.com/workspace/module/view/latest/ri.workshop.main.module.60d62422-3f90-4bfb-8b5e-b33a4ccd966e/main).
2. Click the **New Access Request** dropdown on the right side of the page.
3. Select **Notification Service** from the list.
4. Submit your request.

Approval typically takes a short time. Once approved, you will automatically gain access to:

- The **`notificationLib` Python package** -- for sending emails from Python Transforms.
- The **notification webhook** -- for sending emails from Foundry Action Types (e.g., in Workshop applications).
- **TypeScript helper functions** -- for advanced Action Type integrations that need to resolve Foundry User or Group IDs.

---

## Which Integration Path Should I Choose?

The Notification Service offers two ways to send emails. The right choice depends on your use case:

| | **Python Transforms** | **Action Types (Webhook)** |
|---|---|---|
| **Best for** | Automated emails from pipelines, scheduled jobs, or data processing workflows | Emails triggered by a user clicking a button in Workshop or performing an Ontology action |
| **Who typically uses this** | Data engineers, data scientists | Application builders, Ontology developers |
| **Technical requirement** | Familiarity with Python code in Foundry Code Repositories | Familiarity with Foundry Action Types and (optionally) TypeScript Functions |
| **Recipient resolution** | Automatic -- you can pass email addresses, User IDs, or Group IDs and the library handles the rest | Manual -- the webhook only accepts email addresses; helper functions are provided to resolve IDs |

If you are unsure, **start with the Python library** -- it is the simplest path and handles the most complexity for you.

---

## Option A: Sending Emails from Python Transforms

### When to use this

Use this approach when you want to send emails **automatically as part of a data pipeline**. For example:

- A transform finishes processing and you want to notify the data owner.
- A scheduled job detects an anomaly and should alert the responsible team.
- A periodic report needs to be emailed to a list of stakeholders.

### How it works

After access is granted, the `notificationLib` Python package is available in your Foundry code repository's conda environment. You import the library in your transform code, configure the email parameters (recipients, subject, body, etc.), and the library sends the email for you.

The library supports all recipient types:

- **Email addresses** (e.g., `jane.doe@merckgroup.com`)
- **Foundry User IDs** -- the unique identifier for a Foundry user (a UUID like `f6c84a43-819d-4b81-b137-0af5e20eece2`)
- **Foundry Group IDs** -- the unique identifier for a Foundry Multipass group

When you pass User IDs or Group IDs, the library automatically resolves them to the corresponding email addresses before sending.

For the full list of parameters and detailed code examples, see the [notificationLib README](https://palantir.mcloud.merckgroup.com/workspace/code/repos/ri.stemma.main.repository.f725e281-4987-4574-9c71-3f0f5b983b3f/contents/refs%2Fheads%2Fmaster).

### Token / Authentication

The notification service verifies your identity using a Foundry token. Here is what you need to know:

- **During development and testing**, your personal Foundry user token is sufficient. The service will send emails on behalf of your user session.
- **For production pipelines**, you should use a **Third Party Application (TPA) token** instead of a personal token. Personal tokens are tied to your user account, expire periodically, and need to be renewed manually -- if you forget, your pipeline will stop working. A TPA token is a service account-style credential designed for automated processes that runs independently of any individual user.

#### How to get a TPA token

If you do not already have a TPA for your use case, you can request one by raising a [Foundry Issue](https://palantir.mcloud.merckgroup.com/workspace/issues-app/) with the following information:

1. The **Use Case** the TPA is for.
2. A brief **description** of why you need it (e.g., "Automated email notifications from a scheduled pipeline").
3. Whether you will use the **Ontology SDK**.
4. Who should be the **owner/delegates** (the users who will receive the client ID and client secret).

For detailed instructions and all available options, see the [Developer Console documentation](https://docs.uptimize.merckgroup.com/foundry/beta/beta-applications/dev-console/).

#### Securely storing your token

Never hard-code a token directly in your transform code -- it will be visible in the repository's change history. Instead, use a **Data Connection Source** to store the token securely as a credential, and access it in your code using `source.get_secret()`.

For step-by-step instructions on setting up a Source and storing credentials, refer to the [Foundry Source Setup documentation](https://www.palantir.com/docs/foundry/data-connection/external-transforms#access-source-attributes-and-credentials).

---

## Option B: Sending Emails from Action Types (Webhook)

### When to use this

Use this approach when you want to send emails **in response to a user action** -- for example:

- A user clicks a "Send Notification" button in a Workshop application.
- An Ontology Action is triggered (e.g., when someone approves a request or changes a status).
- A workflow step needs to notify the next person in the process.

### How it works

In this approach, the Notification Service is called via a **webhook** that is configured inside a Foundry Action Type. When the action is executed, the webhook sends an HTTP request to the notification service, which then delivers the email.

### Getting started: The Template Action Type

The easiest way to set up email sending via an Action Type is to start from the existing template:

**[\[MDAO-Template\] Send an email via action](https://palantir.mcloud.merckgroup.com/workspace/ontology/action-type/ri.actions.main.action-type.94106c86-21a3-4555-be21-bb1733409dc8/overview)**

This template shows a fully configured Action Type that sends an email. You can review it to understand the setup, and then **request a copy from the MDAO team** customized for your use case (users cannot duplicate it directly).

For reference, the production Action Type powering the service is: [global-mdao-notification-service-action](https://palantir.mcloud.merckgroup.com/workspace/ontology/action-type/ri.actions.main.action-type.07ad437b-f880-4154-9d69-f01af33de63c/overview)

### Advanced: Custom webhook integration

If you need more control (e.g., dynamically computing recipients or the email body), you can build a custom Action Type that calls the production webhook directly.

**Webhook name:** `global-mdao-notification-service-webhook-prod`

The webhook expects the following data:

```typescript
interface MDAOLibraryWebhookInput {
  emails: string[];      // TO recipients (email addresses only)
  ccEmails: string[];    // CC recipients (email addresses only)
  bccEmails: string[];   // BCC recipients (email addresses only)
  content: string;       // Email body (supports HTML)
  subject: string;       // Email subject line
  topic: string;         // Sender display name shown in email clients
  link: string;          // Legacy -- see note below
}
```

> **Important:** Unlike the Python library, the webhook **only accepts email addresses** -- not Foundry User IDs or Group IDs. If you need to send to User IDs or Group IDs, you must resolve them to email addresses first using the TypeScript helper functions described below.

### TypeScript helper functions

These functions are provided to help you work with User IDs and Group IDs when using the webhook. They are available in your Foundry environment after you have been granted access to the Notification Service.

| Function | What it does |
|----------|--------------|
| `resolveNotificationInputsToMdaoProdWebhookInput` | The **recommended** function for most cases. Pass in any combination of email addresses, User IDs, and Group IDs -- it resolves everything to email addresses and returns the complete payload ready for the webhook. |
| `resolveAllInputsToEmails` | A lower-level helper that just resolves User IDs and Group IDs to email addresses. Use this if you have your own custom TypeScript functions and only need the resolution step. |

---

## Parameter Reference

This section describes all the fields you can use when sending an email. Whether you are using the Python library or the webhook, the core parameters are the same.

### Recipients

You must specify at least one recipient across TO, CC, or BCC.

| Parameter | Type | Description |
|-----------|------|-------------|
| `emails` | List of strings | **TO recipients.** The main recipients of the email. Must be email addresses when using the webhook. The Python library also accepts User IDs and Group IDs via separate parameters (see below). |
| `ccEmails` | List of strings | **CC (Carbon Copy) recipients.** These people receive a copy of the email and can see they were copied. |
| `bccEmails` | List of strings | **BCC (Blind Carbon Copy) recipients.** These people receive a copy but other recipients cannot see that they were included. |

### Email content

| Parameter | Type | Description |
|-----------|------|-------------|
| `subject` | String | **The email subject line.** This is what recipients see in their inbox before opening the email. |
| `content` | String | **The email body.** This is the main content of your email. You can use **HTML** to include rich formatting -- for example, `<b>bold text</b>`, `<a href="...">links</a>`, `<ul><li>bullet lists</li></ul>`, tables, and more. |
| `topic` | String | **The sender display name.** This is what appears as the "from" name in email clients like Outlook. For example, if you set this to `"Data Quality Team"`, recipients will see the email as coming from *"Data Quality Team"* rather than a generic system address. Choose something that helps recipients understand the context of the email. |
| `link` | String | **Legacy field -- not recommended for new use.** If provided, a footer is added to the email saying *"Check it out on UPTIMIZE Foundry"* with a hyperlink to the URL you provide. Since the `content` field supports full HTML, it is better to include any links directly in the email body instead. |

### Additional parameters (Python library only)

When using the `notificationLib` Python package, you can also specify recipients by their Foundry User ID or Group ID. The library will automatically look up the associated email addresses.

| Parameter | Type | Description |
|-----------|------|-------------|
| `userIds` | List of strings | Foundry User IDs for TO recipients. Each ID is resolved to the user's email address. |
| `groupIds` | List of strings | Foundry Multipass Group IDs for TO recipients. Each group is expanded to the email addresses of all its members. |
| `ccUserIds` / `ccGroupIds` | List of strings | Same as above, but for CC recipients. |
| `bccUserIds` / `bccGroupIds` | List of strings | Same as above, but for BCC recipients. |

---

## Restrictions & Limitations

### Internal email addresses only

This service is designed for **internal Merck communication only**. Emails can only be sent to recipients at the following domains:

- `merckgroup.com`
- `emdgroup.com`
- `merck.de`
- `emdserono.com`
- `milliporesigma.com`
- `mtrust.io`
- `external.merckgroup.com`
- `external.milliporesigma.com`
- `external.emdserono.com`

If you try to send to an email address outside of these domains (e.g., a personal Gmail or a vendor's email address), the email will be rejected.

### Maximum 50 recipients per email

A single email can be sent to a maximum of **50 recipients** in total. This limit counts all TO, CC, and BCC recipients combined.

If you do not specify any TO recipient (i.e., you only use CC and/or BCC), the limit is **49** because the service internally adds a placeholder TO address.

> **Watch out when using Group IDs:** When you send to a Foundry Group, the service expands the group into the individual email addresses of all its members. A group with 100 members counts as 100 recipients -- which exceeds the 50-recipient limit. Before sending to a group, make sure the group is small enough. If you need to notify a larger group, consider splitting the send into multiple batches. Support for larger recipient lists is planned for a future release.

### Fixed email template

All emails use the standardized **UPTIMIZE-branded email template**. You cannot use a custom email design, change the logo, or alter the overall layout. The `content` field (which supports HTML) gives you flexibility over the email body, but the surrounding template is fixed.

---

## Frequently Asked Questions

### "My email or action failed -- what went wrong?"

The most common cause is **exceeding the 50-recipient limit**. This often happens when sending to a Foundry Group that has many members. Check the group's membership count in Foundry and consider reducing the audience or splitting into multiple sends.

### "How do I find someone's Foundry User ID?"

A Foundry User ID is a unique identifier (UUID) for each user in the platform. You can find it by navigating to the user's profile page in Foundry -- the User ID is the UUID shown in the URL. For example: `https://palantir.mcloud.merckgroup.com/multipass/user/f6c84a43-819d-4b81-...`

If you need to find User IDs programmatically (e.g., in a transform), you can use the Foundry Multipass API.

### "How do I find a Foundry Group ID?"

A Foundry Group ID is a unique identifier for a Multipass group. You can find it by navigating to the Groups section in Foundry and opening the group -- the Group ID is the UUID in the URL. You can also use the Multipass API to search for groups by name.

### "Can I send to external email addresses?"

No. The Notification Service is restricted to internal Merck email domains only. See the list of [allowed domains](#internal-email-addresses-only) above.

### "Can I customize the email design?"

You cannot change the overall email template or branding. However, you have full control over the email body through the `content` field, which supports HTML. You can use this to format text, add links, insert tables, or include images.

### "Which webhook or action type should I use?"

Use the production webhook: **`global-mdao-notification-service-webhook-prod`**. If you come across references to `global-mdo-notification-service-webhook` (without the "a" in "mdao"), that is an older variant and should no longer be used for new integrations.

### "I need help setting this up -- who do I contact?"

If you need assistance with the Notification Service, reach out to the MDAO team. You can also review the [notificationLib README](https://palantir.mcloud.merckgroup.com/workspace/code/repos/ri.stemma.main.repository.f725e281-4987-4574-9c71-3f0f5b983b3f/contents/refs%2Fheads%2Fmaster) for detailed code examples and configuration guidance.

# Development Guidelines

---

## Browse this section to learn about naming conventions in UPTIMIZE Foundry.

---

## Project Level Permissions
As a Use Case Owner, the projects for Use Case will be set up automatically whenever your Use Case is getting created. If you need a second project for your Use Case, you can use the Project Requestor functionality integrated into the Use Case Portal.  

## Project Requestor
The **Project Requestor** is fully featured in the **Use Case Portal 2.0**, enabling the automation of creating additional projects for Use Cases. When a Use Case is moved to *Proof of Value Development* status, the **Use Case Form** allows users to automatically create a project to store the Use Case outcomes and deliverables. While this is the default method for generating projects, some Use Cases require extra projects.

The process of requesting and assessing additional projects is managed by the corresponding SDOs. The **Project Requestor** automates the request process, allowing use case owners to ask for an extra project under specific conditions. The request is then created and assessed by the SDOs. Once approved, the project creation is automated and linked to the Use Case.

The functionality of the **Project Requestor** is integrated into the **Use Case Portal 2.0** user interface in two different places, catering to the view of the UC owners (requestors) and the SDO group (assessment).

### Requesting projects
The summarized view of a Use Case includes four buttons in the Linked Projects section. These buttons provide the functionality to raise a *UPTIMIZE Request* to a Use Case, such as requesting an additional project.

The visibility of these buttons is based on user roles and conditions regarding the module and the Use Case they refer to.

- **Add Project** (Admins only): This button is used to add or remove projects linked to a Use Case and is available to users who are part of the *project-bootstrapper-viewer* group. Specific conditions apply, including the Use Case status and GxP compliance.

- **Create project** (Admins only): This button links to the *Project Bootstrapper* application and is visible under specific conditions similar to the "Add Project" button.

- **Request Manager** (Request Project): This button triggers the action to create a new project request and is visible to users with specific roles and for Use Cases meeting certain criteria.

- **View Existing Requests** (only visible when you have an active Request): This button allows users to view all project requests linked to a particular Use Case.

### Assessing project requests
SDO members can assess project requests for Use Cases through the **Use Case Portal 2.0 Admin Page**. The page includes a table with information about project requests for Use Cases in a specific business sector. SDOs can manage project requests for their sectors using this page.

### Project Request Object View
The Object View of the **Project Request Object Type** is used by Use Case owners when requesting projects and by SDO members when assessing them.

This view includes the following elements:
- Top toolbar: Buttons for approving, rejecting, and editing the project request, as well as sharing the request.
- Status information section: Image representing the current status and an explanation of it.
- Properties of the Project Request object: Displayed in two subsections.
- Linked Use Case section: Includes the name of the Use Case and a button to display the Use Case details.
- Comments section: Allows users involved in the project request to interact with each other via comments.

### Project Request Shared View in the Use Case Portal 2.0
This page allows users to see the project request object view and is shareable using the address in the URL. Users are redirected to the Admin Page or Personal Page based on their roles over the project request.

Ensure that the Security groups like owner, editor & viewer are set up on the project level by the Sector Data Offices based on the project requirements.  
Further information on permissions best practices is available [here](https://palantir.mcloud.merckgroup.com/docs/foundry/security/projects-and-roles/#sharing-and-moving-resources)

---

# Data Ingestion

## Creating Project Spaces

Make sure you start the implementation of data ingestion request by creating the proper project structure for `raw, transform, ontology` folders through project [bootstrapper](https://wiki.merckgroup.com/confluence/display/DAP/Project+Bootstrapper). If the developer does not have permissions to create project through bootstrapper, the respective supervisor or responsible data steward can create one for the developer.

## Project Naming Conventions

The project created through bootstrapper would automatically create the project space with standard naming conventions like:

| **Project**                                       |**Naming Convention**|
|:--------------------------------------------------------------------|:---------------|
| Data source Project | `sector-name`-data-source-`data-source-name`
| Transform Project |  `sector-name`-transform-`project-name`
| Ontology Project| `sector-name`-ontology-`project-name`
| Use case Project |`sector-name`-use-case-`project-name/use-case-name`

Where `sector-name` takes value as

- ls - Life Science

- hc - Healthcare

- el - Electronics

- mgf - Group Functions

- global - Cross-Sector

The following flow-chart explains when `sector-name` can be classified as global:
![sector name](https://docs.uptimize.merckgroup.com/docs-assets/foundry/Images/Naming_Conventions/Data_Ingestion/sector_name.png)


## Folder Structure

The folder structure within each project should be the created using the project bootstrapper. This creates all the parent folders as below

- analysis - To create any counter analysis

- data - To store outcome of the transformations

- logic - To create project repositories

- playground - To test

The internal or child folders are proposed as per the Foundry recommendations for each project as below

## Data Source Project


| **Folder Name**|**Sub-folder Name**|**Mandatory Usage**|**Purpose**|
|:---------------|:------------------|:------------------|:----------|
|analysis||optional |Resources created to test or document the clean-up transforms and show the shape of the data
|data  | |mandatory |To store outcome of the transformations
|data|raw|mandatory |The landing zone of raw dataset, stores the raw data as ingested from the source system via a Sync
|data |preprocessed |optional|The raw data after pre-processing is stored here (e.g.) Changing source formats from non-readable formats to csv
|data|clean|optional|The clean dataset after pre-processing is stored in this folder
|logic||optional|Includes the Code Repository created by the Project Bootstrapper. Used for data conversion (e.g. JSON to dataset)
|playground||optional|Any temporary resources created in the process of building or testing the cleanup transforms

```
├── analysis
├── data
│   ├── raw
│   ├── preprocessed
│   └── clean
├── logic
└── playground
```

## Transform Project

| **Folder Name**|**Sub-folder Name**|**Mandatory Usage**|**Purpose**|
|:---------------|:------------------|:------------------|:----------|
|analysis||optional |Resources created to test or document the clean-up transforms and show the shape of the data
|data  | |mandatory |To store outcome of the transformations
|data|transformed|mandatory |The data from clean of Data source project is picked and applied business rules/logic and stored here.
|data |output |optional|The clean dataset after applying transformation is stored in this folder
|logic||mandatory|Includes the Code Repository created by the Project Bootstrapper.
|playground||optional|Any temporary resources created in the process of building or testing the cleanup transforms

```
├── analysis
├── data
│   ├── transformed
│   ├── output
├── logic
└── playground
```

## Ontology Project

| **Folder Name**|**Sub-folder Name**|**Mandatory Usage**|**Purpose**|
|:---------------|:------------------|:------------------|:----------|
|analysis||optional |Resources created to test or document the clean-up transforms and show the shape of the data
|data  | |mandatory |To store outcome of the transformations
|data|transformed|optional|The data from multiple transform project is picked and applied business rules/logic and stored here.
|data |ontology |mandatory|The clean dataset after applying transformation is stored in this folder
|logic||mandatory|Includes the Code Repository created by the Project Bootstrapper.
|playground||optional|Any temporary resources created in the process of building or testing the cleanup transforms

```
├── analysis
├── data
│   ├── transformed
│   ├── ontology
├── logic
└── playground

```

│   ├── analysis

# Ontology Objects

## Lifecycle

- Active: Indicates that the object type, property, or link type is actively in use in user-facing applications and major breaking changes will not be made in the Ontology Manager.

- Experimental: Indicates that the object type, property, or link type is still under development. Changes may be made that make the experimental item unavailable in user facing applications.

- Deprecated: Indicates that the object type, property, or link type will soon be deleted. The deprecated item should not be relied on in user facing applications.
  - A deprecated object type, property, or link type also has metadata that includes:
    - A description for why it is being deprecated;
    - A deadline for when it is expected to be deleted from the system; and
    - The object type, property, or link type that is meant to replace the one that is deprecated.

## Naming Conventions

A challenge of the Foundry ontology is that users are exposed to a potentially large amount of objects, actions, and links without the ability to discern their ownership. Even worse, with multiple sectors using the same ontology, naming clashes are bound to happen. Last, but not least, the Foundry Ontology is both: a data model as well as an API definition. We will thus need to adhere to a set of naming conventions in order to ensure managability and auditability of the platform.

## General

For all building blocks of the Ontology, the following names need to be considered:

- Display Name: this is the name that is visible to users. It can be changed after the fact and should not impact any code or applications.
- Id: this is the identifier uniquely determining the object/action/link/property. It is created on object creation and cannot be edited later. Therefore it is impossible to retroactively adapt the ids to changes in the naming standards.
- API Name: this is the name of the object/action/link/property with which it can be referred to in code. An example would be functions used in Actions or Slate Dashboards. This name can be edited, but doing so will break all code referencing the object and thus needs to be done with care.

In the light of the above, the following naming conventions for each of the concepts in the ontology should be adhered to in order to have a general and harmonized approach for creating ontologies.

## Building Blocks

Here are a few building blocks that will be used throughout the document.

### Sector


| **sector**|**sector-short**|**sector-short-upper**
|:----------|:---------------|:--------------------|
| Healthcare |hc|HC 
| Electronics|el|EL 
| Lifescience |ls|LS
| Group Functions|mgf|mgf
|Global|global|Global

## Organizational Unit (ou)
A sector-specific short tag used to indicate teams or larger projects for which it makes sense to group ontology items. An example for Electronics would be the PRIME and BOOST ontologies.

## Hex
To avoid name clashes and for disambiguation a random 3-byte hexadecimal value can be attached to object ids. An example would be `a1b2cd`.

## Global Object exceptions
Global objects are shared across all sectors and are, so-to-speak, the backbone of the ontology. For them the " " prefix in the Display Name is dropped from all concepts.

## Object Types
Objects type represents a class of real-world entity sharing a common set of properties and behaviors. The individual entities are referred to as objects. Somewhat imprecisely the object types are also being referred to as objects and for the sake of simplicity we adopt this convention here to unless it is not clear from the context which meaning is desired. For naming object types we have the goal to on the one hand make the provenance and ownership of an object clear and monitorable and, on the other hand, to maintain simplicity for user facing strings. We thus require the object id and API name to be as specific as possible, while only requiring a sector specified for the display name.

- Display Name: `[<sector-short-upper>] Object Name`
- Id: `<sector-short>-<ou>-object-name-<hex>` (lower-kebab-case)
- API Name:` <sector-short><ou>ObjectName` (UpperCamelCase)

If a qualifier in the display name is desired, add it after the sector name separated by a dash:

- Display Name: `[<sector-short-upper>-<ou>] Object Name`

## Examples

- Display Name: `[EL-Boost] Child Batch`
- Id: `el-boost-child-batch-56fc1a`
- API Name: `ElBoostChildBatch`

In addition, we would like to distinguish use-case specific object names from more reusable objects. Objects defined on datasets located in use-case projects follow the naming convention. Also here we only require the sector name for the display name:

- Display Name: `[<sector-short-upper>-UC-<UC-name-upper-camel-case>] Object Name`
- Id: `<sector-short>-uc-<uc-name>-object-name` (lower-kebab-case)
- API Name: `<sector-short>-uc-<ucid>ObjectName` (UpperCamelCase)

## Examples

- Display Name: `[EL-UC-MyAnalysis] Awesome Object`
- Id: `el-uc-myanalysis-awesome-object`
- API Name: `ElUcMyAnalysisAwesomeObject`

## Object Types Datasets

In addition, there are two properties that can benefit from standardization: the backing and writeback name. Assuming an object is backed by a dataset called `object_name`, then the writeback dataset should be named the same, but receive a `_writeback` suffix.

- Backing dataset: `object_name` (lower_snake_case)
- Writeback dataset: `object_name_writeback` (lower_snake_case)

For clearly expressing that a dataset is used to back an object, we suggest, but do not require, to prefix the dataset with `object_`.

## Examples

- Backing dataset: `object_child_batch`
- Writeback dataset: `object_child_batch_writeback`

## Object Type Properties

Object Type properties represent the characteristics of the individual objects. As they are tightly linked to the object type, there is no need to adorn them with ownership information. However, the default Foundry naming conventions should be followed:

- Display Name: `Object Property Name`
- Property Id: `object-property-name` (lower-kebab-case)
- API Name: `objectPropertyName` (lowerCamelCase)

## Examples

- Display Name: `Material Number`
- Property Id: `material-number`
- API Name: `materialNumber`

## Action Types

Actions define the behavior of the objects. As such they act on at least one object or may even link multiple objects together. Naming them is non-trivial. Good action names are descriptive and allow to determine what primary effect of the action is. In the list below we define normative parts of the naming and then give examples of action names.

- Display Name: `[<sector-short-upper>] `Action Name
- API Name: `<sector-short>-<ou>-action-name-<hex>`(lower-kebab-case)

*Note that the API name specification differs in terms of casing from the other API Names. The reason for this is that Foundry generates new Action Type API Names as lower-kebab-case by default and this convention seems to be used by most existing options. We are thus codifying an already established convention.*

## Examples:

- `el-create-new-material-abcdef`
- `global-set-use-case-owner-a2c124`
- `ls-create-course-registration-b3a1d2`
- `hc-link-drug-to-patient-b3a1d2`

As a general guidance, an action name should express what its effects are (creation, modification, or deletion of objects; creation of links; API calls) as well as the objects it is acting on.

## Link Types

Link types describe the relation between objects. The can be thought of a labelled edge in a graph of objects, with the name of the link type as the label. An important, Foundry-specific consideration is that defining a link type creates methods on the linked object types.

Both linked object types need to be named. Names should express the role of the linked object to the object it is being linked to. API names need to be globally unique and therefore should be as specific as possible.

- Display Name: Descriptive name of the role of the linked object type. Avoid using plain object names.
- API Name: `<sector-short><ou>RoleOfObject` (lowerCamelCase)

## Example

We want to model data ownership of data assets. We have global object types modelling Data Assets and Employees. As Data Owners are always Employees, we choose to simply create a link between Employee and and Data Asset. The names would be the following:

- Employee
   - Display Name: `Data Owner`
   - API Name: `globalDataOwner`
- Data Asset
   - Display Name: `Owned Data Asset`
   - APIName: `globalOwnedDataAsset`

 ## Link Types Datasets

Similar to object types, link types can be backed by a dataset (for many to many relationships). In this case we use a similar naming convention:

- Backing dataset: `link_role_of_source_object_to_role_of_target_object` (lower_snake_case)
- Writeback dataset: `link_role_of_source_object_to_role_of_target_object_writeback` (lower_snake_case)

## Examples

- Backing dataset: `link_child_batch_to_parent_batch`
- Writeback dataset: `link_child_batch_to_parent_batch_writeback`

---

# Coding Standards

The standard language is Python with PySpark. Below are standard guidelines that are expected to be followed by the data ingestion engineers to improve and maintain code quality and make your codebase more stable.

Please also check the Palantir-provided [PySpark Style Guide](https://github.com/palantir/pyspark-style-guide)

## Indentation
Use four spaces per indentation level. Spaces, and not tabs, are the preferred indentation method in Python. Moreover, Python 3 doesn't allow you to mix spaces and tabs for indentation.

**Good practice**

Example: Vertical alignment
```shell
congrats = joining_four_strings_and_repeating_three_times('Happy', 
                                                          'New',  
                                                          'Year',
                                                          '!') 
                                                          `
```


Example: Hanging indent
``` shell
congrats = joining_four_strings_and_repeating_three_times( 
    'Happy', 'New',  
    'Year','!')   
```
**Bad practice**

Bad practice since no arguments are allowed in the first line when using hanging indent.
``` shell
congrats = joining_four_strings_and_repeating_three_times('Happy',  
    'New','Year','!')  
```    
## Maximum line length
According to PEP8 standards, the length of a line should be limited to at most 79 characters. This is because it allows you to have multiple files open next to one another, while also avoiding line wrapping. Docstrings and comments have an even shorter limit and should not exceed 72 characters. However, keeping statements to 79 characters or less is not always possible. This is a recommendation to enhance the readability of your code.

## Note:
Line length does not impact the performance of your code. This is merely for readability and cleanliness. Those particular were also arbitrarily specified by the official style guide for consistency, since many people have different preferences.
## Line Breaks
Long lines can be broken over multiple lines by wrapping expressions in parentheses and using Python's implied line continuation inside parentheses. Backslashes can also be acceptable for breaking up lines, but only in cases when implicit continuation cannot be applied (for example, if you're writing multiple long with statements). For formulas, the best practice is to break lines before binary operators, since this usually results in more readable code. However, it is also permissible to break a line after a binary operator if this matches your local convention.

**Good practice**

Line breaks before binary operators
``` shell
GDP = (private_consumption 
       + gross_investment 
       + government_investment 
       + government_spending 
       + (exports - imports)) 
```
## Bad practice

Line breaks after binary operators
``` shell
GDP = (private_consumption + 
       gross_investment +  
       government_investment + 
       government_spending + 
       (exports - imports))
```       
## Imports
Any Python code should start with imports of necessary libraries and classes. It is recommended to place imports of different libraries on separate lines. However, it is okay to import several classes from the same module in one line.

**Good practice**

Importing different libraries in different lines
```
import numpy 
import pandas 
```
Importing different classes from the same module
```
from sklearn.metrics import confusion_matrix, classification_report
```
**Bad practice**

Importing different libraries in one line
```
import numpy, pandas 
```
## Usage of Python Libraries
Using copy and paste to share substantial bits of code across different projects is bad. Whenever you want to use code or functions across multiple projects/code repositories, put that code into a [Code Repository Library](https://palantir.mcloud.merckgroup.com/docs/foundry/transforms-python/share-python-libraries) and link all your transforms projects against it. Common functions that are used multiple times can be called from a file with utilizes. This can be relaxed for small bits of code.

## Comprehensibility of Code
Follow a few common practices to ensure that your code is easy to understand:

**Comments**: Comments help other developers skim through your code and gain an understanding of how it all works very quickly. You can help ensure a smooth transition by choosing to comment your code from the outset of a project

**How to write Comments?.**

**Basic Comments**

To write a comment in Python, simply put the hash mark # before your desired comment:
```
 # This is a comment
``` 
**Multiline Comments**

While Python doesn't have native multiline commenting functionality, you can create multiline comments in Python. There are two simple ways to do so.

- The first way is simply by pressing the return key after each line, adding a new hash mark (#) and continuing your comment from there.
```
  def multiline_example(): 
    # This is a pretty good example  
    # of how you can spread comments 
    # over multiple lines in Python
```  
Each line that starts with a hash mark will be ignored by the program.

- The second way is use multiline strings by wrapping your comment inside a set of triple quotes.
```
"""
If I really hate pressing `enter` and
typing all those hash marks, I could
just do this instead
"""
```
This is like multiline comments in Java, where everything enclosed in the triple quotes will function as a comment.

**Commenting Best Practices**

Comments are blocks of natural language text in your code.

Always write comments that really support your code.
Shortly describe what you are accomplishing with your following code in mostly non-technical terms.
While it's good to know how to write comments in Python, make sure that your comments are readable and easy to understand.
Add some clarity to complex functions and put a docstring at the top of all your scripts.
Go back and review old code that you've written. See where anything might not make sense and clean up the code. If it still needs some extra support, add a quick comment to help clarify the code's purpose.

**Commenting Bad Practices**

- Avoid: W.E.T. Comments
Your comments should be D.R.Y. The acronym stands for the programming maxim Don't Repeat Yourself. This means that your code should have little to no redundancy. You don't need to comment a piece of code that sufficiently explains itself, like this one as below.
```
return a # Returns a
``` 
We can clearly see that a is returned, so there's no need to explicitly state this in a comment. This makes comments W.E.T., meaning you wrote everything twice.

- Avoid: Smelly Comments
Comments can be a sign of code smell, which is anything that indicates there might be a deeper problem with your code. Code smells try to mask the underlying issues of a program, and comments are one way to try and hide those problems. Comments should support your code, not try to explain it away. If your code is poorly written, no amount of commenting is going to fix it.
```
# A dictionary of families who live in each city 
mydict = { 
    "Midtown": ["Powell", "Brantley", "Young"], 
    "Norcross": ["Montgomery"], 
    "Ackworth": [] 
} 
def a(dict): 
    # For each city 
    for p in dict: 
        # If there are no families in the city 
        if not mydict[p]: 
            # Say that there are no families 
            print("None.") 
```
This code is quite unruly. There's a comment before every line explaining what the code does. This script could have been made simpler by assigning obvious names to variables, functions, and collections, as below.
```
families_by_city = { 
    "Midtown": ["Powell", "Brantley", "Young"], 
    "Norcross": ["Montgomery"], 
    "Ackworth": [], 
} 
def no_families(cities): 
    for city in cities: 
        if not families_by_city[city]: 
            print (f"No families in {city}.") 
```
By using obvious naming conventions, we were able to remove all unnecessary comments and reduce the length of the code as well.

Your comments should rarely be longer than the code they support. If you're spending too much time explaining what you did, then you need to go back and refactor to make your code clearer and more concise.

**Speaking Variables:**

Jupyter Notebooks are meant to be shared and read by others. Others might not have an easy time following our thought process, if we did not come up with self-explaining variable names. Therefore, use self-explanatory code to name your variables. In addition, use some way to indicate the spaces between words in variable names. Since you can't use actual spaces, some common ways to do this.
```
feature_names =['num_cylinders','length','weight'] 
target_name = ['mpg'] 
 
features = data[feature_names] 
target = data[target_name] 
 
clf = DecisionTreeClassifier() 
clf.fit(features, target) 
```
## Correctness
**Code Tests:** Ensure that your code is correct. By correct we mean that your code does what you say/think it does. This can be tricky to check. One way to make sure your code is correct is through code review. You can help make sure your code is correct by including some tests. Tests are little pieces of code you use check that your code is working correctly. They improve confidence in the correctness of data manipulations, data cleaning and the data output. Make sure to create [unit tests](https://palantir.mcloud.merckgroup.com/docs/foundry/transforms-python/unit-tests) for the most essential parts of your complex logic.

**Code Reviews:** Code reviews are a software quality assurance activity in which at least one individual check a code asset by viewing and reading its source code. The goal of code reviews is to ensure that any new code that's being integrated into a repository is free of bugs, secure, follows established coding and formatting conventions and is comprehensible.

## Configurable Parameters
Making all parameters in your analysis configurable has several benefits. You can quickly adjust components by changing a central parameter section or file without going through the whole code. Furthermore, your collaborators and users can make substantial modifications without changing the underlying code.
```
N_JOBS = 1 
CLASSIFIER_MODULE: "sklearn.ensemble" 
CLASSIFIER_NAME: "RandomForestClassifier" 
N_ESTIMATORS: 2000 
MAX_DEPTH: 5 
RANDOM_STATE: 24 
```
## Modularity
Modular components are easier to reuse, debug, and extend. Ensure that key parts of your pipeline (e.g., data sourcing, preprocessing, transformations) are standalone. Sample modules can include the following.

**Source:** Pipe data from multiple original sources into a single database or separate files (e.g., images for training a classifier).

**Preprocess:** Read from the database, use asserts to validate key staged data properties, engineer features, transform, and save to files.

## Efficiency
**DRY Principle (to be followed):**

Apply the DRY principle (Don't repeat yourself), which is a principle of software development aimed at reducing repetition of software patterns. Adhering to the DRY principle increases efficiency. The DRY principle is stated as "Every piece of knowledge must have a single, unambiguous, authoritative representation within a system".

**WET Principle (to be avoided):**

The opposite of DRY code is called WET (We enjoy typing or Wasting everyone's time). WET means writing the same code or logic again and again. Therefore, it will be difficult to manage the code and if the logic changes, then one needs to make changes in all the places where the code was previously written.

# Pipeline Quality & Maintenance Best Practices

## Health Checks & Monitoring:

Scheduling the Pipelines for builds and configuring the health checks and monitoring the configured health checks is a key practice to
determine the data health and eventually quality of the data.

Foundry application allows users to configure technical "Health Check" on a dataset. Data Health is a Foundry service that monitors and alerts on common issues across datasets.
Data Health comes with pre-built health checks for potential issues regarding dataset categorized into status, time, size, content, and schema.


## Global Checks:

Data Health is integrated with the Foundry Notifications service and Foundry Email service to provide in-platform notifications and emails, respectively, in the event of a failed check. There are in total 26 available health checks broken down into 5 categories.

Find more details to all available Health Checks on the [Foundry Documentation](https://palantir.mcloud.merckgroup.com/docs/foundry/data-health/checks-reference).

# Global Health Checks Configuration Best Practices

## Project Locations - Global Health Checks:

Global health checks are applicable to -data-source and -ontology projects


| Project |Folder Structure|
|:--------|:---------------|
|`<sector>-data-source-xxxxx` |clean,<br><br> Analysis<br><br> logic<br><br> `data/raw` -> The mandatory/optional health checks (if applicable) can be configured to the appropriate datasets of this folder    
|`<sector>-ontology-xxxxx` | logic<br><br> `data/ontology` -> The mandatory /optional health checks (if applicable) can be configured to the appropriate datasets of this folder.

# Data Quality Checks
## Data Source Project
### Data Storage In Raw
Initially, data ingested from external sources needs to be stored in a designated and access-controlled namespace. The ingestion of data will aim at the `/raw` area.

### Data Quality Verification In Raw
After ingestion is completed and data is stored in `/raw`, data quality needs to be verified to confirm a successful transfer. This indicates an equivalent copy of the data from the source system into Foundry.

### Minimum Checks
For any ingestion, the following should be checked on a minimum level. To this end, some typical objectives of the `raw → clean` transformation include:

- Imposing a consistent column naming scheme

- Ensuring appropriate types are applied to columns

- Handling missing, malformed, or mis-typed values

- Convert empty string to Null

- Establishing primary Data Health checks - Data count, column count, column order etc.

- Removing PII or other sensitive data unsuitable for general consumption

### Data Standardization
Convert data format with Integers or Double that are empty to '0' or '0.000'

Convert Boolean Values as True/False.

Convert Date Values to Standardized format.

## Transform Project
In the transformation layer, the following should be checked on a minimum level:

- Deduplication of the data

- Data Format checks

- Size check (column and row count)

Perform Project specific transformations to create reusable canonical datasets. It is recommended to use Pyspark in Foundry Code Repositories for transformations. For spark optimizations please refer the [link](https://palantir.mcloud.merckgroup.com/docs/foundry/optimizing-pipelines/usage-optimization/#step-7-optimize-spark)

# Code Quality
Quality code consists of those features that cater to the need of customers and subsequently provide product satisfaction. We can take the "freedom from deficiencies" as referring to the non-functional attributes required in code. Some of these non-functional attributes are:

- **Clarity** - Easy to read and legible to anyone who has not written the code themselves.
- **Maintainability** - An easy to understand the context and not being over-complicated makes it easier to make changes in the code if required.
- **Documentation** - While well-written code explains itself, it is always recommended to add comments to explain the role it plays and its functions.
- **Refactored** - The formatting needs to be consistent and follow relevant coding conventions.
- **Well-tested** - The smaller number of bugs a software has, the higher its quality is. Rigorous testing helps filter out such discrepancies.
- **Efficiency** - High-quality code uses the resources available to its optimally to perform the desired action.

# Branching Guidelines

Often more than one person is working on a pipeline. For productive pipelines an environment is needed in which changes can be introduced without impacting any users or downstream transformations. Branching is the solution two both issues. In this document we are focusing on the conventions that should be adhered to, in order to safely collaborate and introduce changes into existing code bases with confidence. We will assume that you have a working knowledge of the branching concepts available in Foundry. Therefore please read the following documents first:

- [Branching](https://palantir.mcloud.merckgroup.com/docs/foundry/data-integration/branching)
- [Fallback Branches](https://palantir.mcloud.merckgroup.com/docs/foundry/data-integration/branching)

Note that each sector may have different or additional practices in addition to what is being described here. For global repositories that are being worked on across sectors, following the branching scheme described here becomes mandatory.

## Creating branches
Each code repository should have at least two protected branches: `master` and `development`. These two branches are protected, meaning that changes can only be introduced via pull request and a subsequent code review. The purpose of this is to ensure a stable state of the production pipeline (`master`) and a mostly stable branch to develop against collaboratively (`development`). The second, important, reason to have a development branch everywhere is that the dataset fallback logic works across projects: transformations on the development branch of project B, automatically pick up datasets on the development branch of project A. This means that a consistent, non-production chain of transformations can be established.

## Merging
In order to introduce changes into a branch, we are using pull requests. After a pull request has been created, it is reviewed and merged into the target branch. For merging, it is crucial that the correct merge strategies are being used in order to preserve a sane commit topology and create a clear development history.

Below we have a list of branches with their respective merge strategies attached:

- **master**: The main production branch. Changes to this branch affect the data end-users are seeing in their analyses. No changes are done to the `master` branch that have not first been tested in the development branch. The `master` branch must be protected! Merging strategy to merge development into `master`: Please only use `Merge`! When you use `Merge` in a pull request, all the individual commits created on the branch will be added to the target branch alongside a merge-commit.
- **development**: The main development branch. It is created from `master` and is merged back once a set of changes has been reviewed and is ready to be released to production. Although it is a development branch, it should always be kept stable to provide a solid foundation for downstream consumers. The guideline is to merge `development` into `master` relatively frequently to avoid that the two branches diverge too much. The `development` branch should be protected! Merging strategy to merge a feature branch into master: Please only use `Squash & Merge`! `Squash & Merge` mode will create a single commit to the target branch incorporating all the changes that the pull request introduces. Contrary to other development contexts where it is preferred to keep all commits, Foundry creates many auxillary and automatic commits that are not useful. By squashing, the commit history gets summarized to a single, more understandable commit. As a direct consequence, only use a feature branch to develop a single logical piece of code and merge back as soon as possible.
- **Feature branches**: These are where the actual development happens and are usually owned by a single developer working on a task. They are always created from `development` and merged back after development has concluded and they are ready to be reviewed. To simplify the review process (and for reasons stated above), they should be kept small and have a 1:1 relationship to a work item. Naming convention for feature branches (please use lower-case!): `dev/<sector>/<ticket-no>_<feature_title>`. Optionally, the developer owning the feature branch can be added to the name: `dev/<sector>/<developer>/<ticket-no>_<feature_title>`. For non-global branches with clear SDO responsibility, the sector name can be omitted from the branch name.

## Branching Schematic

Feature branches should be deleted after they were merged into development. As a general rule, code only flows from a branch to the branch directly above it. There must not be any backward merges (e.g. master → validation)!

**Note**: If code got merged to a specific branch, the merging developer must build all datasets, which are impacted, in the respective branch! Similarly, prior to submitting a pull request, ensure that all datasets on the merged branch have been built such that reviewers can assess the impact of the change.

---

# Code Review Guidelines


## Code Reviews
The recommended approach for code reviewing on LHS is Peer Review Approach. The code review is initiated through a Pull Request of the feature branch to the master branch in the source code repository. The purpose is to ensure that the necessary quality in the software development activities are achieved, the guidance laid out in this document and referenced documents are followed and best practices in terms of software engineering are met. The source code review needs to be conducted from a data engineer not involved in the development of that component.

## Peer Review
Code reviews/Peer review are important because they improve code quality and make your codebase more stable. Benefits of code/ Peer reviews:

- Improved code quality
- Fewer defects in code
- Improved communication about code content
- Education each other and build relationships
- Work more effectively

## Approach & Guidelines
- The developers are expected to create a review group for the sector data ingestion teams. The group names have been documented here.

   `<sector-data-ingestion-engineers>`


- New developers are also to be included in this group.

- Once the team receives a pull request notification from a member of the group, it's expected that a team initiates the review process.

- Below are the checklist/criteria, the code is expected to meet.

- The team is encouraged to communicate with each other on the code/ review content, using the Conversation tab on the Pull request


## Check List for Code Review

| Criteria                                                        Y/N |
|---------------------------------------------------------------------|
|Is the master branch locked and dev branch created?                  |
|Is the code understandable?                                          |
|Are all the requirements implemented?                                |
|Are the naming conventions followed?                                 |
|Are the coding guidelines followed?                                  |
|Does the code contain necessary commenting?                          |
|Is there any duplication of code?                                    |
|Will your code handle bigger data in future ?                        |

## Communication Through Conversation tab
![communication through conversation tab](https://docs.uptimize.merckgroup.com/docs-assets/foundry/Images/Development_Guidelines/Code_Review_Guidelines/conversation_tab.png)

It is highly recommended to establish a healthy and constructive conversation as the code review process is ongoing, using the conversation tab on the pull request. This practice benefits the understanding of on the code and methods of implementation both to the requester and the reviewer.
