# DuckDB as Compute Engine in Foundry

This guide highlights how [Apache Spark](https://spark.apache.org/) jobs can be migrated to [DuckDB](https://duckdb.org/) to achieve up to 90% runtime and compute cost savings for small to medium size datasets.

## Introduction

### DuckDB

DuckDB is a fast, in-process, analytical, open-source, portable database system. It excels at executing SQL queries on small to medium size (<500GB) datasets, with performance competitive to the fastest Cloud Data Warehouses (e.g. Snowflake) on the market. DuckDB has a state of the art out-of-core query engine that can spill to disk for larger-than-memory processing. Paired with instances with local NVMe SSDs a Container with DuckDB is consistently able to complete queries with dataset sizes larger then the available memory.

### Parquet and DuckDB

Parquet files are compressed columnar files that are efficient to load and process. DuckDB provides support for both reading and writing Parquet files in an efficient manner, as well as support for pushing filters and projections into the Parquet file scans. In most cases, these parquet files are stored on cloud object stores, like the Foundry Filesystem. While Spark does support Parquet, the execution engine of Spark is inherently row-based. This means that the data from Parquet needs to be converted at least once to a row-based in-memory format which limits the efficiency that can be achieved. DuckDB, with its native, columnar design does not share these limitations and thus benefits from lower overhead while loading data and can exploit vectorized computation during execution.

### Sidecarless Lightweight & S3 Proxy

The Foundry PD Team created a new [lightweight](https://palantir.mcloud.merckgroup.com/docs/foundry/transforms-python/lightweight-overview/) architecture that exposes a AWS S3-compatible API Proxy (S3 Proxy) to the compute engines running in the lightweight container. The S3 Proxy handles the interaction with the Foundry Filesystem as well as caching and other optimizations. This implementation does not suffer from the limitations of the previous implementation where the "sidecar" container quickly became the bottleneck for any data-heavy operations and prevented efficient partial reads of Parquet files. As a consequence, by using a fast, embedded engine such as DuckDB (but also Polars), further execution time savings compared to Spark can be achieved.

![Architecture](assets/foundry/lightweight-v2-architecture.png)

DuckDB natively supports the S3 API via the offical [httpfs](https://duckdb.org/docs/stable/core_extensions/httpfs/overview.html) extension and thus can access data on Foundry without the need for additional, custom code or extensions.

## Considerations
In the following section, we discuss some of aspects of using DuckDB effective and efficiently. As with all engineering topics, some trade-offs need to be considered in order to pick the most appropriate solution design.

## When to use?
DuckDB is an engine focused on single-node execution. While not being able to scale beyond a single node may seem like a big limitation, in practice very few transformations require more resources than a single node can handle. For example, the maximum size of a lightweight transform in Foundry is [8 CPU cores and 64GB of memory by default](https://palantir.mcloud.merckgroup.com/docs/foundry/transforms-python/lightweight-api#resource-provisioning). This limit is increased on our stack to 25 CPU cores and 192GB of memory. This is many times the size of most datasets on Foundry. This means that using DuckDB is, in general, an option that is worth considering.

However, please note that the size of datasets is only an indicator for the eventual performance. Joins resulting in a large number of rows, or which expand the number of columns significantly can increase the memory requirements dramatically. If this is the case, Spark is still a reliable tool to complete the workload without hard-to-debug failures.

Next, consider that DuckDB support in Foundry is currently very immature. There is no IDE support (code hints, debugger) and thus, developing reliable code can become more difficult than in Spark. Also, the current libraries provided by the MDAO team are based on unstable features, meaning that future upgrades of code using DuckDB are to be expected. Therefore it is currently recommended to implement DuckDB only in cases where significant cost savings can be realized. We expect this limitation to be much less relevant in the future, though.

## Tuning
Below are some tuning guidelines to maximize the performance per dollar spent which we *strongly* suggest should be your key metric for optimization.

**Memory is king:** DuckDB (as most compute engines) benefits from being able to keep its data in memory. Computational speed will slow down considerably once spilling to disk comes into the picture. In cases where a dataset can be streamed and processed in chunks, there should be no impact on performance. High-complexity queries that required the computation of large intermediate results may need to persist these to disk and read them later if the lightweight transform has too little memory available. Consider increasing the available memory and measure. If query performance is poor, double the available memory and try again. As increases are non-linear, you may need to spend some time searching for a sufficient amount. Note down your measurements and pick the most optimal cost / resource ratio. If memory needs to be increased by 4x to achieve a speedup of 10x, then this is a net positive!

**CPU to memory ratio:** From the billing perspective the ideal CPU to memory ratio is [7.5 GiB per core](https://palantir.mcloud.merckgroup.com/docs/foundry/resource-management/usage-types/#memory-to-core-ratio) as this grants the most resources for spent money. However, consider that running multiple parallel threads may actually compete for memory and thus lead to a non-linear speedup or even worse compute speeds in case of overcontention. Therefore, it is sometimes worth to overprovision memory to ensure that a working set fits into memory. As results should be written per thread to maximize throughput, using more cores can also result in more and smaller Parquet files, which may be detrimental for downstream transforms. Therefore, consider carefully whether increasing CPU in lockstep with memory is providing actual benefits. Alternative, you can set the number of worker threads that DuckDB will use through a [global configuration option](https://duckdb.org/docs/stable/configuration/overview.html#global-configuration-options).

## Migration from PySpark

## SQLFrame

[SQLFrame](https://github.com/eakmanrq/sqlframe/tree/main) is an open-source library that implements the PySpark DataFrame API to enable running transformation pipelines directly on database engines like DuckDB without requiring Spark clusters or dependencies. From our tests it's the most complete and stable PySpark compatibility layer on the market.

### SQLFrame Capabilities

SQLFrame is an open-source library that implements the PySpark DataFrame API to enable running transformation pipelines directly on database engines like DuckDB without requiring Spark clusters or dependencies. From our tests it's the most complete and stable PySpark compatibility layer on the market. The maintainer has been very responsive to contributions from MDAO colleagues and quickly provides bugfixes when issues are identified.

Key benefits of SQLFrame include:

* Using a DataFrame API that leverages the full power of the underlying database engine
* Running PySpark code locally without the overhead of starting a Spark session
* Generating SQL representation of DataFrame code for debugging or sharing
* Running PySpark DataFrame code without the complexity of Spark processing

SQLFrame can be configured to generate human-readable SQL when using `df.sql(optimize=True)`, and it supports different SQL dialects for input and output to make it feel more like a native DataFrame API for your chosen engine.

## Required Dependencies

To use DuckDB in Foundry lightweight transforms, you need to add the following dependencies to your project. These can be added through the Code Repository's Library panel. Afterwards, the dependency section of your `meta.yaml` file should include:

```yaml
- foundry-transforms-lib-python
- python-duckdb 1.4.1
- arbor-duckdb-extension-httpfs-1-4-1
- sqlframe
- transforms-duckify >=0.3.12
```

These dependencies provide:
- **foundry-transforms-lib-python**: Core Foundry transformations library
- **python-duckdb 1.4.1**: The DuckDB engine itself
- **arbor-duckdb-extension-httpfs-1-4-1**: Extension for DuckDB to interact with remote file systems via HTTP/S3
- **sqlframe**: PySpark-compatible API for DuckDB
- **transforms-duckify**: Utilities to integrate DuckDB with Foundry transforms, maintained by the MDAO team

> **Important**: The version of `arbor-duckdb-extension-httpfs-1-4-1` must match the version of `python-duckdb`. For example, `python-duckdb 1.4.1` requires `arbor-duckdb-extension-httpfs-1-4-1`. If the DuckDB version is upgraded in the future, a corresponding extension version will be needed. The MDAO team is responsible for providing and maintaining the `arbor-duckdb-extension-httpfs` library after DuckDB upgrades, ensuring compatibility across versions. Always check with the MDAO team for the appropriate extension version when upgrading DuckDB.

Make sure these dependencies are properly configured before running the examples below.

## Examples for Using DuckDB in Foundry

### Example 1: Direct SQL Query with DuckDB

This is the basic approach for using DuckDB in Foundry. This example demonstrates:
- Using the `get_files_literal` function to access Foundry datasets as file paths
- Creating a DuckDB session with `get_sqlframe_duckdb_session`
- Writing a direct SQL query that references Parquet files
- Using `write_table` to save results back to Foundry

```python
from duckdb import DuckDBPyConnection
from sqlframe.duckdb.session import DuckDBSession, DuckDBDataFrame
from sqlframe.base import functions as F
from transforms.api import (
    Input,
    LightweightInput,
    LightweightOutput,
    Output,
    lightweight,
    transform,
)
from transforms_duckify._darkwing import (
    get_files_literal,
    get_sqlframe_duckdb_session,
    write_table,
    as_duckdb_frame,
)


@lightweight(cpu_cores=2, memory_gb=15)
@transform(
    flights=Input("ri.foundry.main.dataset.d5308d45-9822-4e02-afb9-2704636308ee"),
    transforms_output=Output(
        "ri.foundry.main.dataset.4880fd72-faea-4fd6-b440-7a84d2eb76bb"
    ),
)
def compute_sql(flights: LightweightInput, transforms_output: LightweightOutput):
    # Returns a SQLFrame DuckDBSession
    session = get_sqlframe_duckdb_session()

    # Returns string array of format ["s3://.../file1.parquet", "s3://.../file1.parquet"]
    # This can be directly used in the read_parquet call.
    flights_parquet_files = get_files_literal(flights)

    sql_query = f"""
        SELECT
            *
        FROM
            read_parquet({flights_parquet_files})
        WHERE
            origin = 'ATL'
    """

    write_table(session, transforms_output, sql_query)
```

### Example 2: Using DuckDBPyConnection to hand-craft statements

This example demonstrates:
- Creating a SQL view from a Parquet files
- Accessing the `DuckDBPyConnection` object directly to execute DDL statements
- Using standard SQL syntax against the registered view

```python
@lightweight(cpu_cores=2, memory_gb=15)
@transform(
    flights=Input("ri.foundry.main.dataset.d5308d45-9822-4e02-afb9-2704636308ee"),
    transforms_output=Output(
        "ri.foundry.main.dataset.227f451b-1204-4cbe-8aa7-82f1b9e809c8"
    ),
)
def compute_sql_with_view(
    flights: LightweightInput, transforms_output: LightweightOutput
):
    # Returns a SQLFrame DuckDBSession
    session = get_sqlframe_duckdb_session()
    # Returns a DuckDBPyConnection, can be used for registering views etc.
    ddb_connection = session._conn

    # Returns string array of format ["s3://.../file1.parquet", "s3://.../file1.parquet"]
    # This can be directly used in the read_parquet call.
    flights_parquet_files = get_files_literal(flights)

    ddb_connection.execute(
        f"CREATE VIEW flights AS SELECT * FROM READ_PARQUET({flights_parquet_files})"
    )

    sql_query = """
        SELECT
            *
        FROM
            flights
        WHERE
            origin = 'ATL'
    """

    write_table(session, transforms_output, sql_query)
```

### Example 3: SQLFrame with SQL Views

This example shows:
- Using `transforms_duckify.as_duckdb_frame` function to create a dataframe and register a view automatically
- Accessing the registered view name through the `latest_cte_name` property
- Using the view name in a SQL query

```python
@lightweight(cpu_cores=2, memory_gb=15)
@transform(
    flights=Input("ri.foundry.main.dataset.d5308d45-9822-4e02-afb9-2704636308ee"),
    transforms_output=Output(
        "ri.foundry.main.dataset.f23019e3-9542-47e1-8559-2fc50624ed50"
    ),
)
def compute_sql_with_view_sqlframe(
    flights: LightweightInput, transforms_output: LightweightOutput
):
    # Returns a SQLFrame DuckDBSession
    session = get_sqlframe_duckdb_session()

    # as_duckdb_frame generates a view with name ri_foundry_main_dataset_d5308d45_9822_4e02_afb9_2704636308ee
    # the view name can be accessed with flights_df.latest_cte_name
    flights_df = as_duckdb_frame(session, flights)

    # Use in hand-written SQL to query View
    sql_query = f"""
        SELECT
            *
        FROM
            {flights_df.latest_cte_name}
        WHERE
            origin = 'ATL'
    """

    write_table(session, transforms_output, sql_query)
```

### Example 4: Full SQLFrame API Usage

This example demonstrates the power of SQLFrame's PySpark-compatible API, which allows for:
- Converting Foundry datasets to SQLFrame DataFrames using `as_duckdb_frame`
- Using PySpark-like syntax with the SQLFrame `functions` module (imported as `F`)
- Filtering data using a familiar DataFrame API instead of writing raw SQL
- Writing the resulting DataFrame directly to the output using `write_table`

> **Important**: SQLFrame and PySpark imports cannot be mixed and matched in the same file. You must choose one or the other for your entire script. If you try to import both in the same file, you may encounter namespace conflicts and unexpected behavior.

```python
from duckdb import DuckDBPyConnection
from sqlframe.duckdb.session import DuckDBSession, DuckDBDataFrame
from sqlframe.base import functions as F
from transforms.api import (
    Input,
    LightweightInput,
    LightweightOutput,
    Output,
    lightweight,
    transform,
)
from transforms_duckify._darkwing import (
    get_files_literal,
    get_sqlframe_duckdb_session,
    write_table,
    as_duckdb_frame,
)

@lightweight(cpu_cores=2, memory_gb=15)
@transform(
    flights=Input("ri.foundry.main.dataset.d5308d45-9822-4e02-afb9-2704636308ee"),
    transforms_output=Output(
        "ri.foundry.main.dataset.b73a4565-f7db-4ea1-bbc8-2ff4444de878"
    ),
)
def compute_with_sqlframe(
    flights: LightweightInput, transforms_output: LightweightOutput
):
    # Returns a SQLFrame DuckDBSession
    session = get_sqlframe_duckdb_session()

    flights_df = as_duckdb_frame(session, flights)

    # Use PySpark compatibility syntax
    output_df = flights_df.filter(F.col("origin") == "ATL")

    write_table(session, transforms_output, output_df)
```

### Example 5: Using a Temporary Directory for Iterative Rule Execution

This example demostrates to write results to an intermediate temp directory for iterative query executions, and read recursively from that path to populate final output

This example demonstrates how the assignment engine processes a series of region- and team-specific rules directly against operational and transactional tables in DuckDB, rather than pre-aggregating or transforming them in a heavy distributed system like Spark.
Each rule is executed independently, and the matching records are written into an intermediate temporary directory as Parquet files. 
The folder structure is organized by region and component (e.g., temp/EMEA/SALES_EMEA/iter_0001.parquet) so each execution's output is isolated for debugging, audit, and partial re-runs.

After all rules have been executed:
- The engine recursively reads all Parquet files from the temporary directory.
- Deduplication logic ensures only the first matching rule per record is kept.
- A final consolidated output table is produced for downstream processes.

**Important**: DuckDB with temp staging avoids distributed-system overhead and can execute iterative rule logic significantly faster and with fewer resources.

Example Rules Table Content: 
| component_id | iter_id | is_active | rule_set_id | region_key | condition |
|--------------|---------|-----------|-------------|------------|-----------|
| SALES_EMEA   | 1       | FALSE     | RS1001      | EMEA       | CASE WHEN country IN ('DE','AT','CH') AND annual_revenue > 100 THEN 'T1' END |
| SALES_EMEA   | 2       | FALSE     | RS1001      | EMEA       | CASE WHEN country IN ('GB','IE') AND employee_count >= 50 THEN 'T2' END |
| SALES_NA     | 1       | FALSE     | RS2001      | NA         | CASE WHEN state IN ('CA','WA','OR') AND arr_estimate > 100 THEN 'T3' END |
| SALES_NA     | 2       | FALSE     | RS2001      | NA         | CASE WHEN state IN ('TX','OK') AND arr_estimate BETWEEN 50 AND 100 THEN 'T4' END |
| SALES_APAC   | 1       | FALSE     | RS3001      | APAC       | CASE WHEN country IN ('JP','KR') AND annual_revenue > 5000 THEN 'T5' END |
| SALES_APAC   | 2       | TRUE      | RS3001      | APAC       | CASE WHEN territory_code IS NOT NULL THEN territory_code END |


```python
@lightweight(cpu_cores=2, memory_gb=15)
@transform(
    out=Output(output_location),
    transactions=Input(processed_product_matrix_rid),
    rules=Input(rules_prepared_rid),
)
def compute(
    out: LightweightOutput,
    transactions: LightweightInput,
    rules: LightweightInput
):
    # Create DuckDB session
    session = get_sqlframe_duckdb_session()

    # Materialize inputs inside DuckDB (1:1 with your code)
    tra_all_df = as_duckdb_frame(session, transactions, True)
    rules_df   = as_duckdb_frame(session, rules, True)

    # Temp dir for parquet outputs
    with tempfile.TemporaryDirectory() as tmpdir:
        tmpdir = Path(tmpdir)

        # Create temp table for candidates
        session._execute(f"""
            CREATE OR REPLACE TEMP TABLE tra_all_temp AS
            SELECT * FROM {tra_all_df.latest_cte_name}
        """)

        # Filter active and ordered rules
        rules_df = rules_df.filter(F.col("is_active")).orderBy("component_id", "iter_id")

        # Iterate rules and copy queries to parquet: tmp/{region_prefix}/{component_id}/iter_{iter_id}.parquet
        for row in rules.collect():
            comp_id   = row["component_id"]
            iter_id   = row["iter_id"]
            region_prefix = row["region_id"]
            condition = row["condition"]

            out_dir  = tmpdir / region_prefix / str(comp_id)
            out_dir.mkdir(parents=True, exist_ok=True)
            out_file = out_dir / f"iter_{iter_id:04d}.parquet"

            wrapped_sql = f"""
                WITH candidates AS (
                  SELECT *
                  FROM tra_all_temp
                  WHERE
                    territory_code IS NULL
                    AND array_contains(component_id, '{comp_id}')
                    AND region_key = '{region_prefix}'
                ),
                evaluated AS (
                  SELECT
                    group_identifier,
                    rule_set_id,
                    region_key,
                    ({condition}) AS entry_territory_code
                  FROM candidates
                )
                SELECT
                  '{comp_id}' AS component_id,
                  group_identifier,
                  {iter_id}   AS iteration,
                  rule_set_id,
                  region_key,
                  entry_territory_code
                FROM evaluated
                WHERE entry_territory_code IS NOT NULL
            """

            # here we persist the resulting parquet to our temp directory
            session._execute(f"COPY ({wrapped_sql}) TO '{out_file.as_posix()}' (FORMAT parquet, PER_THREAD_OUTPUT TRUE)")

        # Drop temp table of candidates, we don't need anymore
        session._execute("DROP TABLE IF EXISTS tra_all_temp")

        # Build final union straight from a glob
        # Keep the first match per (component_id, group_identifier) by iteration asc
        query_from_temp = f"""
            SELECT component_id, group_identifier, iteration, rule_set_id, region_key, entry_territory_code
            FROM (
              SELECT
                component_id,
                group_identifier,
                iteration,
                rule_set_id,
                region_key,
                entry_territory_code,
                ROW_NUMBER() OVER (
                  PARTITION BY component_id, group_identifier
                  ORDER BY iteration
                ) AS rn
              FROM read_parquet('{(tmpdir / region_prefix / "**" / "*.parquet").as_posix()}', union_by_name = true)
            )
            WHERE rn = 1
        """
        write_table(session, out, query_from_temp)
```

## Understanding write_table Logging

The `write_table` function not only writes your data to the specified Foundry output dataset but also provides valuable logging functionality:

1. **Build Log**: The SQL query is logged to the build log, making it easy to see what SQL was actually executed during your transformation. Long SQL Queries might be truncated due to limitations with the Logging infrastructure in Foundry.

2. **Hidden File**: Additionally, `write_table` creates a hidden file in the output dataset named `_/_ddb_sql_query.txt` that contains the final SQL query that was executed. This is particularly useful for:
   - Debugging complex transformations
   - Understanding how SQLFrame expressions were translated to SQL
   - Optimizing queries by examining the actual SQL that DuckDB executed
   
To access this hidden file, you can navigate to your dataset in Foundry and use the file browser to locate the `_` directory, which contains the `_ddb_sql_query.txt` file.

### Install git (if you haven't already)

To be able to version control our code or interact with any code stored on Foundry we need `git`.

><details open>
><summary><b>on macOS</b></summary>
>
>To check if git is already installed, run in a terminal:
>
>```bash
>git version
>```
>On a fresh install of macOS, you should see the below note. You can install git together with the Xcode Command Line Tools 
>via the system dialog which should open and prompt you to download and install the tools. 
>
>```bash
>xcode-select: note: No developer tools were found, requesting install.
>If developer tools are located at a non-default location on disk, use 'sudo xcode-select --switch path/to/Xcode.app' to specify the Xcode that you wish to use for command line developer tools, and cancel the installation dialog.
>See 'man xcode-select' for more details.
>```
>
>Otherwise, if `git version` does not return a git verison number such as e.g. `git version 2.50.1`, you can choose one of the installation options (Homebrew, Ports, XCode,...) from [here](https://git-scm.com/download/mac).
>
></details>


><details open>
><summary><b>on Windows</b></summary>
>
>Download git from [here](https://git-scm.com/download/win)
>
>When following the installation wizard, please select these two options:
>
>You need to select Windows Secure Channel Library backend, this will trust the certificates from Windows,
>otherwise git won't work with the ssl-inspection mentioned in [Set up the Merck SSL Certificates](#set-up-the-merck-ssl-certificates).
>
>![git schannel](https://docs.uptimize.merckgroup.com/docs-assets/foundry/Images/foundry-dev-tools/git_schannel.png)
>
>You should also select "Git from the command line and also from 3rd-party software",
>otherwise you will only be able to use git, in the git bash and not in cmd.
>And you should select "Git Credential Manager" when asked.
>
>If you couldn't select the option, or hadn't the chance to select it,
>you will need to set these settings in the shell, like so:
>
>```bash
>git config --global http.sslbackend schannel
>git config --global credential.helper manager
>```
>
></details>

### Miniforge / Mambaforge Installation

We recommend to install Mambaforge on Windows and Miniforge on MacOS/Linux as a package manager for python packages.
It is conda drop-in replacement in C++ and it uses the `conda-forge` channel by default.

<b>If you have anaconda installed, please uninstall it, it will interfere with mambaforge</b>

Anaconda changed their ToS in 2020 and are asking commercial users to purchase one of
their [commercial offerings](https://www.anaconda.com/blog/anaconda-commercial-edition-faq).

By using Mambaforge in combination with conda-forge we do not have to worry about licensing since they are offered
under the BSD-3-Clause license. For more reading on this topic, I can recommend
this [blog post](https://florianwilhelm.info/2021/09/Handling_Anaconda_without_getting_constricted/)



#### If you had anaconda installed previously

Before you uninstall anaconda, run this command in your Terminal:
```shell
conda config --remove-key channels
```
This should remove the anaconda channels (with the licensing issue) from the configuration file in your home directory.
If you already have anaconda uninstalled or the command won't run, you can just the delete the .condarc file in the home directory:

><details open>
><summary><b>on macOS</b></summary>
>
>```shell
>rm ~/.condarc
>```
></details>

><details open>
><summary><b>on Windows</b></summary>
>
>CMD:
>```shell
>del %USERPROFILE%\.condarc
>```
>
>PowerShell:
>```shell
>rm $env:USERPROFILE\.condarc
>```
>
></details>


Then uninstall anaconda, it depends on how you installed it, you may need to contact IT to remove it for you.

### Set up the Merck SSL Certificates

When you are connected to the Merck Network, either via VPN or in the Office, the network traffic gets SSL inspected and
re-encrypted using a Merck owned SSL Certificate.  
Command line tools like Conda or pip use the python requests library
internally, which **does not support using the system trust store** but rely on `certifi` to provide certificates
(for more background reading on the topic have a look at this [GitHub Issue](https://github.com/psf/requests/issues/2966)).

So, in order to allow these tools to communicate with the repositories, we need to tell them to use a trust store with
the Merck certificates included.

#### Download cacert.pem file

<i>Connect to the VPN or connect with the corporate network, if you previously disconnected from the VPN or connected with a hotspot in the office.</i>

Now, download the cacert.pem file from the following Foundry resource [here](https://palantir.mcloud.merckgroup.com/workspace/preview-app/ri.blobster.main.certificate.ab5dc834-157d-46e0-9efc-b3757b973585),
and place it in `MAMBA_PATH`

#### Set the cacert.pem location in the .condarc file

In the miniforge prompt execute:

```bash
conda config --set ssl_verify MAMBA_PATH\cacert.pem
```

#### Setup cacert.pem in pip.ini


><details open>
><summary><b>on macOS</b></summary>
>
>
>Run this command in your shell:
>
>```bash
>MAMBA_PATH/bin/pip config set global.cert MAMBA_PATH/cacert.pem
>```
>
></details>

><details open>
><summary><b>on Windows</b></summary>
>
>Run this command in miniforge:
>
>```bash
>cd MAMBA_PATH
>python.exe -m pip config set global.cert MAMBA_PATH\cacert.pem
>```
>
>#### Fix Certificate issues on Windows
>
>On Windows there is an issue with the schannel library (the Windows specific TLS library) that Mamba uses.
>To mitigate this issue you'll need to set the `MAMBA_SSL_NO_REVOKE` environment variable.
>
>To do this: 
>First open up the same windows as in [the steps above](#add-mambaforge-to-the-path-environment-variable).
>
>![](https://docs.uptimize.merckgroup.com/docs-assets/foundry/Images/foundry-dev-tools/edit_env_vars.png)
>
>Click on New (1.), and then use as a Variable Name (2.) `MAMBA_SSL_NO_REVOKE` and as a value (3.) `1` then press Ok (4.)
>
></details>


### Setup a Conda environment and install the foundry-dev-tools package

For the purpose of this documentation we are going to create a dedicated conda environment called `foundry-dev-tools` in
which we will install `git` as well as `pyspark` and other required packages. If in the future you start to work on different projects with different package requirements it is recommended to create environments per project.

Use the Terminal/iTerm (zsh) (macOS) or the miniforge prompt (windows) to run these commands.

Create an environment called foundry-dev-tools, with the python version 3.12 or any other version support by foundry-dev-tools.

To see the supported python versions of foundry-dev-tools go to [our PyPI project](https://pypi.org/project/foundry-dev-tools) scroll down and on the left side you should see the supported versions, currently (26th August 2024) these are:

![](https://docs.uptimize.merckgroup.com/docs-assets/foundry/Images/foundry-dev-tools/supported_python_versions.png)

```bash
mamba create -n foundry-dev-tools python=3.12 -y
```

Followed by activating the environment.

```bash
mamba activate foundry-dev-tools
```

If you now run `python` you should see an output text similar to this:

```bash
> python
Python 3.x.y | packaged by conda-forge ...
>>>
```

You can exit the python shell with `exit()`.

If it doesn't show the correct python version or does not show conda-forge,
try to restart your shell and run the activate command again.

From now on the path to the environment will be called `ENV_PATH` in our documentation.
You will need to replace it with the path of your environment.

You can find out the path of your environment with:

```bash
conda env list
```

The path with the `*` in front of it, is your currently active environment.
To continue with the installation of Foundry DevTools, you'll first need to setup your environment.

### Install the foundry-dev-tools library

In your conda environment run

```bash
mamba install foundry-dev-tools -y
```

You'll also need to install `foundry-dev-tools-transforms` if you intend to run Foundry transforms locally:

```bash
mamba install foundry-dev-tools-transforms -y
```


### Configuration of foundry-dev-tools

In your terminal with the environment activated type the following command:

```bash
fdt config edit
```

It will show you a prompt like this:   

![](https://emdgroup.github.io/foundry-dev-tools/_images/fdt_config_edit_example.svg)

Select one of the "User config file" options, by typing in the number and pressing enter.
This will open the config file in your default editor.

Create a new token for your Foundry account [here](https://palantir.mcloud.merckgroup.com/workspace/settings/tokens)
and copy it to the clipboard. The token has an expiration date which you can set in the field 'Valid until'
(a reasonable value is a validity for 3 months).
This token acts like a username and password combined, therefore:

**DO NOT SHARE THIS TOKEN WITH ANYONE.**

![](https://docs.uptimize.merckgroup.com/docs-assets/foundry/Images/foundry-dev-tools/tokenFoundry.png)

In the opened editor window, paste the following initial configuration and replace `TOKEN` with your clipboard content:

```toml
[credentials]
domain="palantir.mcloud.merckgroup.com"
jwt="TOKEN"
```

Save the file, and you are ready to connect to Foundry.

### Test Foundry Connection

In the Terminal enter the following command:

```bash
fdt info
```
This will show you an overview of your current environment and config like this, and tell you if you've successfully authenticated with Foundry.  

![](https://emdgroup.github.io/foundry-dev-tools/_images/fdt_info_example.svg)

Please also take a look at the [public Foundry DevTools Documentation](https://emdgroup.github.io/foundry-dev-tools/getting_started/cli.html)


## Setup for legacy local transform development

<b> Palantir has introduced an official method to develop & preview transforms code locally with the [Palantir extension for Visual Studio Code](https://www.palantir.com/docs/foundry/palantir-extension-for-visual-studio-code/overview) . Going forward you should use this as the support within foundry-dev-tools is not maintained. </b>

<details open>
<summary>Legacy Setup Instructions below:</summary>
<br/><br/>
<b>Setup Apache Spark</b>
<br/><br/>
As on Foundry, we will use Apache Spark for data processing, but this time we run it locally.
Conda-forge contains an excellent `pyspark` distribution, which includes not only the python packages of Spark, but
also the java parts.
<br/><br/>
<b>Install openjdk and pyspark</b>
<br/><br/>
Since most parts of Spark are written in Scala and compiled to Java code, we need to
install [openjdk](https://anaconda.org/conda-forge/openjdk) aswell.

In your shell run:

```bash
mamba install openjdk=17 "pyspark<4" -y
```

It is important to install a java version which is supported by your pyspark version. As of now, Foundry uses pyspark 3 (version 3.5.5.25) 
for which the latest compatible java version is 17.

After the installation, the environment variable `JAVA_HOME`
should be present in your terminal. You can check the value with:

><details open>
><summary><b>on macOS</b></summary>
>
>```bash
>echo $JAVA_HOME
>````
>
>Which should return `ENV_PATH/lib/jvm` for MacOS.
></details>


><details open>
><summary><b>on Windows</b></summary>
>
>```bash
>echo %JAVA_HOME%
>````
>
>Which should return `ENV_PATH\Library` for Windows.
></details>
<br/><br/>
<b>Setup Spark configuration file</b>
<br/><br/>
This configuration will also give more memory to pyspark, which otherwise is very limited.

Assuming your laptop has 16GB input the following:

```bash
spark.driver.memory=12g
spark.driver.maxResultSize=5G
spark.sql.execution.arrow.pyspark.enabled=true
spark.sql.broadcastTimeout=9999
spark.driver.extraJavaOptions="-Dio.netty.tryReflectionSetAccessible=true"
spark.executor.extraJavaOptions="-Dio.netty.tryReflectionSetAccessible=true"
spark.driver.host=127.0.0.1
spark.sql.legacy.timeParserPolicy=legacy
```

If you have less memory, adjust `spark.driver.memory`  
and `spark.driver.maxResultSize` accordingly.

Open the file with your editor of choice and paste the above configurations into it adapted to your needs.

><details open>
><summary><b>on macOS</b></summary>
>
>```bash
>cd $CONDA_PREFIX/lib/python3.10/site-packages/pyspark
>mkdir conf
>cd conf
>touch spark-defaults.conf
>```
>
></details>


><details open> 
><summary><b>on Windows</b></summary>
>
>In the Miniforge Prompt:
>
>```bash
>cd %CONDA_PREFIX%\Lib\site-packages\pyspark
>mkdir conf
>cd conf
>notepad spark-defaults.conf
>```
>
></details>
<br/><br/>
<b>Setup winutils/hadoop and pyspark environment variables (Windows only)</b>
<br/><br/>
><details open> 
><summary><b>on Windows</b></summary>
>
>Some hadoop native binaries are not bundled with pyspark, we need to download them separately.
>(More on this issue, [here](https://github.com/conda-forge/pyspark-feedstock/issues/34))
>Download this [winutils.exe](https://github.com/cdarlint/winutils/raw/master/hadoop-3.3.6/bin/winutils.exe) and
>[hadoop.dll](https://github.com/cdarlint/winutils/raw/master/hadoop-3.3.6/bin/hadoop.dll).
>
>The same directory where you created `conf` already had a directory called `bin`, place this file inside of `bin`.
>
>The directory should be:
>
>```bash
>cd %CONDA_PREFIX%\Lib\site-packages\pyspark\bin
>```
>
>We also need to setup pyspark environment variables correctly,
>for this we need to place an activate and deactivate script in the conda environment, otherwise it can't find spark and hadoop.
>
>In your miniforge prompt with the environment activated type:
>
>```bash
>cd %CONDA_PREFIX%\etc\conda\
>dir
>```
>
>There are two directories activate.d and deactivate.d
>
>Go into the activate.d directory and create a new bat fiel called `pyspark_activate.bat`.
>
>```bash
>cd activate.d
>notepad pyspark_activate.bat
>```
>
>Fill it the following content (you normally don't need to change anything in there):
>
>```bash
>@echo off
>set "SPARK_HOME_CONDA_BACKUP=%SPARK_HOME%"
>set "SPARK_HOME=%CONDA_PREFIX%\Lib\site-packages\pyspark"
>set "HADOOP_HOME_CONDA_BACKUP=%HADOOP_HOME%"
>set "HADOOP_HOME=%SPARK_HOME%"
>set "PYSPARK_PYTHON_CONDA_BACKUP=%PYSPARK_PYTHON%"
>set "PYSPARK_PYTHON=%CONDA_PREFIX%\python.exe"
>set "PATH_CONDA_BACKUP=%PATH%"
>set "PATH=%HADOOP_HOME%\bin;%PATH%"
>```
>
>Go into the deactivate.d directory and create a new bat file called `pyspark_deactivate.bat`.
>
>```bash
>cd ..
>cd deactivate.d
>notepad pyspark_deactivate.bat
>```
>
>With the following content (you normally don't need to change anything in there):
>
>```bash
>@echo off
>set "PATH=%PATH_CONDA_BACKUP%"
>set "PATH_CONDA_BACKUP="
>set "SPARK_HOME=%SPARK_HOME_CONDA_BACKUP%"
>set "SPARK_HOME_CONDA_BACKUP="
>set "HADOOP_HOME=%HADOOP_HOME_CONDA_BACKUP%"
>set "HADOOP_HOME_CONDA_BACKUP="
>set "PYSPARK_PYTHON=%PYSPARK_PYTHON_CONDA_BACKUP%"
>set "PYSPARK_PYTHON_CONDA_BACKUP="
>```
>
>Close all miniforge prompts completely, otherwise the environment variables won't get set correctly.
>Open miniforge prompt again, and activate the environment again.
>
>To check that it worked all three of these should output a path and `SPARK_HOME` and `HADOOP_HOME`,
>should be the same directory, where you earlier created the `conf` directory.  
>`PYSPARK_PYTHON` will point to your python.exe
>
>```bash
>echo %SPARK_HOME%
>echo %HADOOP_HOME%
>echo %PYSPARK_PYTHON%
>```
>
></details>
<br/><br/>
<b>Test Spark installation</b>
<br/><br/>
To test if Spark is set up correctly, start a `python` shell from the Miniforge Prompt end execute the following lines:

```python
from pyspark.sql.session import SparkSession
from pyspark.sql.functions import udf, pandas_udf
from pyspark.sql.types import IntegerType
import pandas as pd
spark = SparkSession.builder.master("local[*]").appName("foundry-dev-tools").getOrCreate()
df = spark.createDataFrame(data=[[1, 2]], schema="first: int, second: int")


@udf(IntegerType())
def multiply(value):
    return value*10


@pandas_udf(IntegerType())
def to_upper(s: pd.Series) -> pd.Series:
    return s*20

df = df.withColumn('python_udf', multiply("first"))
df = df.withColumn('pandas_udf', to_upper("second"))

print(df.toPandas())
```

This is the expected output. You can ignore the `WARNINGs`.

```
   first  second  python_udf  pandas_udf
0      1       2          10          40
```

Congratulations! You have succesfully installed pyspark and ran your first spark transform on your machine!

</details>


## Setup Pycharm IDE

If you don't already have PyCharm installed:

Download Pycharm Community Edition from [here](https://www.jetbrains.com/de-de/pycharm/download/other.html).
When opening the installation file, a dialogue asking for elevated credentials will open. Click on `No` and the
installation will continue with user privileges. Install Pycharm to the suggested Path. It is recommended to restart after the install.

When opening Pycharm for the first time you can create the default example project.
As the interpreter you need to select the created foundry-dev-tools environment.
Select "Previously configured interpreter", then "Add Interpreter".
In the popup select "Conda Environment" on the left side.
As the "Conda Executable" if it doesn't find it automatically, select the path

- `MAMBA_PATH\condabin\conda.bat` on windows.
- `MAMBA_PATH\condabin\conda` on macos.

Your environments should load, and now you can select "foundry-dev-tools" in "Use existing environment".
(Further information in the [Jetbrains Documentation](https://www.jetbrains.com/help/pycharm/conda-support-creating-conda-virtual-environment.html#conda-requirements))

Now PyCharm will create your new project, and select "foundry-dev-tools" as your interpreter.

In the future when you work with Python projects, it is important to select the correct Interpreter, in the lower right corner of the window.
(Further information in the [Jetbrains Documentation](https://www.jetbrains.com/help/pycharm/configuring-python-interpreter.html#add-existing-interpreter))

### Set Environment variables and Terminal (Windows only)
><details open>
><summary><b>on Windows</b></summary>
>PyCharm does not respect the environment variables set in the scripts we created [earlier](#setup-winutilshadoop-and-pyspark-environment-variables-windows-only).
>
>You will also need to use cmd.exe, as powershell has some issues with conda, and even tripped my antivirus.
>
>Go to settings (Ctrl+Alt+S), Tools -> Terminal -> And set Shell Path to `C:\Windows\system32\cmd.exe`
>
>You will need to set these globally.
>
>You need to create 4 environment variables:
>
> - FOUNDRY_DEV_TOOLS_ENV: set here your `ENV_PATH` (which you got [here](#create-a-conda-environment))
> - HADOOP_HOME: set it to `%FOUNDRY_DEV_TOOLS_ENV\Lib\site-packages\pyspark`
> - SPARK_HOME: set it to `%HADOOP_HOME%`
> - PYSPARK_PYTHON: set it to `%FOUNDRY_DEV_TOOLS%\python.exe`
>
>You can see these values also in this screenshot:
>
>![edit environment variables for pycharm on windows](https://docs.uptimize.merckgroup.com/docs-assets/foundry/Images/foundry-dev-tools/edit_env_vars.png)
>
></details>

### Verify that it works

You may clone the following [Foundry sample repository](https://palantir.mcloud.merckgroup.com/workspace/data-integration/code/repos/ri.stemma.main.repository.dbbd1d38-6ec8-4458-8c1b-5e70425cc4f1/contents/refs%2Fheads%2Fmaster)
into your Pycharm Projects folder. Go to the link, copy the git repo link (top right corner `Clone`).
Go to the Miniforge Prompt, navigate to the folder where you want to store your projects and run the `git clone`
command.

In Pycharm, click on File -> Open and select the cloned folder, confirm with OK:
Select the transforms-python/src directory, rightclick and mark it as `Sources Root`.

(Further information in the [Jetbrains Documentation](https://www.jetbrains.com/help/pycharm/configuring-project-structure.html#mark-dir-settings))

The sample repository needs an additional dependency (`openpyxl`), open the Terminal in Pycharm,
verify that the correct conda environment is activated (`mamba env list` should indicate foundry-dev-tools with \*).
Now run `mamba install openpyxl -y`

Open the file `transforms-python/src/myproject/datasets/examples.py`, right click and select Run 'examples':

![](https://docs.uptimize.merckgroup.com/docs-assets/foundry/Images/foundry-dev-tools/pycharmRunExamples.png)

## Setup Visual Studio Code IDE

VSCode is nowadays a popular open source IDE with good Python support.  
Download and install VSCode from the [official website](https://code.visualstudio.com/Download).
Open VSCode and install the Python plugin from the Extension Marketplace:

![](https://docs.uptimize.merckgroup.com/docs-assets/foundry/Images/foundry-dev-tools/vscode2.png)

### Configure global settings (Windows specific)

We want "Command Prompt" as our default terminal.

Press `Ctrl + Shift + P`, this will open the command palette. Now search "Open User settings (JSON)" and press enter.

Paste in the following configuration and save:

```json
{
  "terminal.integrated.defaultProfile.windows": "Command Prompt"
}
```

### Configure workspace settings

You may clone the
following [Foundry sample repository](https://palantir.mcloud.merckgroup.com/workspace/data-integration/code/repos/ri.stemma.main.repository.dbbd1d38-6ec8-4458-8c1b-5e70425cc4f1/contents/refs%2Fheads%2Fmaster)
into your VSCode Projects folder as a sample project for the project
settings. You will have to make this configuration for every repository that you use.

Go to the link, copy the git repo link (top right corner Clone).
Go to the Miniforge Prompt, navigate to the folder where you want to store your projects
and run the git clone command.

Open the folder with VSCode, open the command palette (Shift+CTRL+P) and look for `Python: Select Interpreter`.
Select the python binary from the conda repository:

Now, use the command palette to open the workspace settings (search for `Open Workspace Settings (JSON)`) and paste the
following settings (change ENV_PATH):

```json
{
  "terminal.integrated.env.windows": {
    "PYTHONPATH": "${workspaceFolder}/transforms-python/src"
  },
  "python.analysis.extraPaths": ["${workspaceFolder}/transforms-python/src"],
  "python.defaultInterpreterPath": "ENV_PATH/bin/python.exe"
}
```

We are letting VSCode know here that the source root is located in `${workspaceFolder}/transforms-python/src`.

If you have not installed `openpyxl` as part of the PyCharm setup above, open an integrated terminal (hint: use the
command palette).
Verify that the correct conda environment is activated (`mamba env list` should indicate foundry-dev-tools with \*).
Now run `mamba install openpyxl -y`

### Run example

Open the file `transforms-python/src/myproject/datasets/examples.py`, right click and select Run Python File in
Terminal.

## Setup JupyterLab

It's very simple. In Miniforge Prompt, activate the environment und install `jupyterlab`.

```bash
mamba activate foundry-dev-tools
mamba install jupyterlab -y
```

Afterwards, go to your Project directory and start `jupyter lab`:

```bash
cd C:\Users\M99999\PycharmProjects
jupyter lab
```

Jupyter lab will open in your standard browser, and you can create a new Notebook.

With Jupyter Notebooks, you have one **big** advantage -> Spark will stay running, otherwise everytime you re-run your code, spark needs to start again.

# GxP in Foundry

## What's GxP?

GxP, which stands for Good x Practice, is a general term encompassing various regulations, standards, and guidelines that ensure the quality and compliance of products and processes e.g. in Life Science and Healthcare. The "x" represents a specific area of practice, such as manufacturing, laboratory work, or distribution.

The "GxP"-org in Foundry permits use cases that require GxP-standards to be carried out in Foundry.

This allows business users to use results of use cases for their decision making in a fully GxP-compliant way.

In layperson terms, GxP enforces strict traceability of all decisions as well as strong quality assurances processes enforced by a change request management system.

This allows a sufficiently high level of control of all business- and IT processes.


### GxP-relevance for data

All data (datasets and DTIs) that:

* are generated, recorded, or processed during GxP regulated activities
* encompass raw data, processed data, analytical results, quality control data, and GxP relevant documentation
* are designed to be used for GxP purposes

are susceptible to be **GxP-relevant**.

For DTIs there is a flag `GxP relevant` in Foundry. The tagging of DTIs falls under the responsibility of data stewards.

Please see our [guidelines how to access a GxP DTI](https://docs.uptimize.merckgroup.com/foundry/foundry-gxp/access-gxp-dti/).


### Further questions

Please reach out to the the compliance team for any futher questions: [gxp-mdao@merckgroup.com](mailto:gxp-mdao@merckgroup.com).

---

# GxP Architecture

Foundry has both a GxP and a non-GxP instance, called "orgs" in Palantir terms:

* non-GxP Foundry
* GxP Foundry

The use case portal and data treasury are part of non-GxP Foundry: GxP use cases can be requested via the non-GxP org's use case creation portal.

In the future, all GxP Data will be moved to the GxP instance. All data in GxP Foundry is visible and read-accessible to non-GxP Foundry and vice-versa.

---

# GxP Assessment

A formal GxP self-assessment is recommended to classify a use case to be either GxP or non-GxP. 

In the following cases, a GxP assessment is **mandatory**:

* all GxP Use cases,
* all use cases requesting acccess to a GxP relevant DTI,
* all use cases requesting a data ingestion for GxP relevant data.

The GxP assessment consists of completing a [form](https://mango.merckgroup.com/cara/lnk/content/mango_document/2342733/Effective?format=pdf) that must be approved and signed within ManGO.

---

# Access a GxP DTI

> **Note:**<br> This is the procedure to access a GxP DTI from a non-GxP use case. In case you want to ingest a GxP DTI for
your GxP use case, please contact the Foundry OPS team.

To request access to GxP-relevant data treasury items (DTI) from Non-GxP use cases, an owner of the use case must complete specific fields in the UPTIMIZE Use Case Portal to verify their Non-GxP status. One such mandatory field is the `ManGo ID` of the GxP Assessment created for the Use Case. This procedure outlines the steps to create the formal GxP Assessment and properly fill the ManGo ID.

## Steps to register a ManGo ID

### 1.	Create and sign a GxP Assessment

*	Download word template of GxP assessment from [here](https://mango.merckgroup.com/cara/lnk/content/mango_document/2342733/Effective).
*	Finally, get the assessment reviewed by your respective quality department and uploaded & signed in Mango 
* Quality department contacts:
  * Life Science: Anbukumar Marimuthu
  * Healthcare R&D: Alexander Kunz
  * Healthcare GHO: Olivier Schneider 
  * Enabling Functions: Vetiola Elezkurtaj 
  * If applicable, you can also connect on this with your default (local) Quality unit

### 2. Open the Use Case 

* 	Access the [Use Case Portal](https://palantir.mcloud.merckgroup.com/workspace/module/view/latest/ri.workshop.main.module.cfbd54d9-5f5e-4c67-a94d-37fc842812cc/personal). 
* Locate and select the specific use case for which you want to add the ManGo ID.

### 3. Edit Technical Details

* In the use case overview, find the "Technical Details" section.
*	Click the "Edit" button to enable editing of the technical details.

<img src="/assets/foundry/gxp/edit-uc.png">

### 4. Complete mandatory fields

*	Review the mandatory fields within the form. Ensure that all required information is accurately filled out. This may include fields such as use case title, description, and other relevant technical specifications.

### 5. Enter ManGo Id

* Locate the field labelled `ManGo ID of the GxP Assessment created for the Use Case`. 

<img src="/assets/foundry/gxp/uc-form.png">

*	Input the 8-digit ManGo ID associated with your GxP assessment. Ensure that the ID is entered correctly to avoid any issues.
*	The Mango Document ID can be found in the lower left corner of any effective Mango document


<img src="/assets/foundry/gxp/mango-id.png">


### 6. Justification for Non-GxP Use Cases

*	If you select "No" for the "Use Case is GxP?" field based on the outcome of the GxP Assessment, you must provide a justification for the non-GxP relevance in the specified field.
*	Clearly articulate the rationale for why the use case does not fall under GxP. This justification is mandatory for non-GxP assessments and should explain the context and importance of the use case.

### 7.	Save Changes

*	After populating the necessary fields, scroll down and click the "Save" button to apply all changes made to the use case.

### 8.	Submit Access Request
*	Following the successful saving of changes, you can now proceed to submit your access request. Ensure that all components of your request are in order, as this will facilitate a smoother approval process.


By following these detailed steps, you will effectively add the ManGo Document ID of the GxP Assessment to your use case, ensuring compliance and readiness for DTI access requests.

If you face any issues during the process, please reach out to: [gxp-mdao@merckgroup.com](mailto:gxp-mdao@merckgroup.com).

---

# SAP Tempo P70 Automated Checks POC -- Technical Documentation

> **Note:** As of January 2026 the project is in a POC stage. Most of the code is still present on `dev`.

> **Repository:** `tempola-p70-automated-checks`
> **Foundry Path:** `/Global/global-data-source-sap-p70/logic/scratch/tempola-p70-automated-checks`
> **Purpose:** Automated data quality validation between SAP P70 (via Oracle/JDBC) and Snowflake, running inside Palantir Foundry.

---

## Table of Contents

1. [Overview](#1-overview)
2. [Architecture & Data Flow](#2-architecture--data-flow)
3. [Module Reference](#3-module-reference)
   - 3.1 [datasets/tables_to_sync.py](#31-datasetstables_to_syncpy--table-scope-definition)
   - 3.2 [datasets/available_vts.py](#32-datasetsavailable_vtspy--virtual-table-registry)
   - 3.3 [datasets/sync_metadata.py](#33-datasetssync_metadatapy--sap-metadata--oracle-query-builder)
   - 3.4 [datasets/automate_syncs.py](#34-datasetsautomate_syncspy--automated-sync-creation)
   - 3.5 [datasets/job_batches/config_class.py](#35-datasetsjob_batchesconfig_classpy--job-configuration-dataclass)
   - 3.6 [datasets/job_batches/batches/batch_1.py](#36-datasetsjob_batchesbatchesbatch_1py--batch-1-configuration)
   - 3.7 [datasets/job_batches/merged_configs.py](#37-datasetsjob_batchesmerged_configspy--merged-configuration-registry)
   - 3.8 [datasets/comparison/sync_sf_and_compare.py](#38-datasetscomparisonsync_sf_and_comparepy--sap-vs-snowflake-comparison-engine)
   - 3.9 [datasets/combine_comparison_results.py](#39-datasetscombine_comparison_resultspy--results-aggregator)
4. [Data Files (JSON)](#4-data-files-json)
5. [How to Add a New Table for Comparison](#5-how-to-add-a-new-table-for-comparison)
6. [Key Datasets & RIDs](#6-key-datasets--rids)

---

## 1. Overview

This repository implements an **automated data validation pipeline** that compares SAP P70 data (accessed via Oracle JDBC) against the same tables replicated into Snowflake. The pipeline ensures data integrity across the two systems by performing three categories of checks:

| Check Type | Description |
|---|---|
| **Schema Comparison** | Validates that SAP column types (from DD03L metadata) map correctly to Snowflake types. |
| **Row Count Comparison** | Compares total row counts between the SAP source and the Snowflake replica. |
| **Sample Row Comparison** | Takes a deterministic sample of rows from SAP and performs an anti-join against Snowflake to find missing/mismatched records. |

Results are written as JSON per table and then aggregated into a single combined output dataset.

---

## 2. Architecture & Data Flow

```
┌──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│                        FOUNDRY PIPELINE                          │
│                                                                  │
│  ┌───────────────┐    ┌──────────────────┐    ┌────────────────┐ │
│  │ DD03L Dataset │──▶ │  sync_metadata   │──▶ │ Oracle Queries │ │
│  │  (SAP schema) │    │  (metadata +     │    │ (per table)    │ │
│  └───────────────┘    │   query builder) │    └────────┬───────┘ │
│                       └──────────────────┘             │         │
│                                                        ▼         │
│  ┌──────────────┐    ┌──────────────────┐    ┌─────────────────┐ │
│  │ tables_to_   │──▶ │ automate_syncs   │──▶ │ JDBC Synced     │ │
│  │ sync (scope) │    │ (creates syncs)  │    │ SAP Samples     │ │
│  └──────────────┘    └──────────────────┘    └────────┬────────┘ │
│                                                       │          │
│  ┌──────────────┐                                     ▼          │
│  │ available_   │    ┌──────────────────┐    ┌─────────────────┐ │
│  │ vts (VT RIDs)│──▶ │ sync_sf_and_     │──▶ │ Per-Table JSON  │ │
│  └──────────────┘    │ compare (engine) │    │ Results         │ │
│                      └──────────────────┘    └────────┬────────┘ │
│  ┌──────────────┐                                     │          │
│  │ Job Batch    │──▶  (config for each table)         ▼          │
│  │ Configs      │   ┌──────────────────────────────────────────┐ │
│  └──────────────┘   │     combine_comparison_results           │ │
│                     │     (aggregated tabular output)          │ │
│                     └──────────────────────────────────────────┘ │
└──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
```

---

## 3. Module Reference

---

### 3.1 `datasets/tables_to_sync.py` -- Table Scope Definition

**Path:** `transforms-python/src/myproject/datasets/tables_to_sync.py`

**Purpose:** Defines the list of SAP P70 table names that should be synchronized via JDBC. This list is consumed by `automate_syncs.py` when creating new Foundry dataset syncs.

**Key Details:**
- The `tables` list is a plain Python list of SAP table name strings.
- **Currently active tables:** `EKET`, `LIPS`, `AFPO`, `A017`
- Many additional tables are present but **commented out** -- these represent the broader P70 scope in Snowflake (as of October 2025).

**Usage:** Imported by `automate_syncs.py`:
```python
from .tables_to_sync import tables
```

**How to modify scope:** Uncomment or add table names to the `tables` list to include them in the next sync run.

---

### 3.2 `datasets/available_vts.py` -- Virtual Table Registry

**Path:** `transforms-python/src/myproject/datasets/available_vts.py`

**Purpose:** A dictionary mapping SAP P70 table identifiers (e.g., `"P70.EKET"`) to their corresponding Foundry **Virtual Table (VT) RIDs**. Virtual Tables provide Snowflake access via the Foundry Tables API.

**Structure:**
```python
vts = {
    "P70.MAST": "ri.tables.main.table.ff16e262-...",
    "P70.EKET": "ri.tables.main.table.47119ebf-...",
    ...
}
```

**Key Details:**
- Contains **19 entries** mapping SAP table names to VT RIDs.
- Tables marked with `##` in the source comments are the ones that have been prioritized or validated.
- This file is a **reference/lookup** -- it is not directly imported by any transform but serves as documentation of available Snowflake-backed virtual tables.

---

### 3.3 `datasets/sync_metadata.py` -- SAP Metadata & Oracle Query Builder

**Path:** `transforms-python/src/myproject/datasets/sync_metadata.py`

**Purpose:** A **lightweight Polars transform** that reads SAP DD03L metadata (data dictionary) and generates per-table metadata including primary keys, schema definitions, and optimized Oracle sampling queries.

**Transform Type:** `@transform.lightweight` (Polars-based, single-node)

**Resources:** 2 CPU cores, 15 GB memory

**Inputs:**

| Parameter | Dataset | Description |
|---|---|---|
| `dd03` | `ri.foundry.main.dataset.ca4aed25-ff07-4db4-beb6-75336bc31b9f` | SAP DD03L (Data Dictionary: Table Fields) |

**Output:**

| Parameter | Dataset | Description |
|---|---|---|
| `out` | `ri.foundry.main.dataset.7354d0e3-73e4-4bf7-94dd-f1aeba3a2bdd` | Table metadata with Oracle queries (branch: `dev`) |

**Processing Steps:**
1. **Filter DD03L** -- Keeps only rows for the specified `available_vts` tables, active versions (`AS4LOCAL == "A"`), and non-empty datatypes.
2. **Build hash pieces** -- For each column, constructs `COALESCE(TO_CHAR("COLUMN"), '')` expressions.
3. **Identify primary keys** -- Extracts columns where `KEYFLAG == 'X'` and builds a concatenated `ORA_HASH` expression.
4. **Generate schema JSON** -- Serializes column metadata (name, datatype, length, position, keyflag) per table.
5. **Build Oracle sampling query** -- Constructs a deterministic sampling query that:
   - Embeds a `SAP_TOTAL_ROW_COUNT` subquery
   - Uses `ORA_HASH` on primary keys for deterministic row selection
   - Pre-filters to ~50% of rows, then limits to 20,000 sample rows

**Output Schema:**

| Column | Type | Description |
|---|---|---|
| `table_name` | String | SAP table name |
| `source_name` | String | Always `"p70"` |
| `pk_columns` | List[String] | Primary key column names from DD03L |
| `pk_hash_expr` | String | `ORA_HASH` concatenation expression |
| `schema_json` | String (JSON) | Full DD03L-derived column metadata |
| `normalized_select_sql` | String | `SELECT * FROM "SAPP70"."TABLE"` |
| `oracle_query` | String | Complete Oracle sampling query with row count |

**Configuration Constants:**

| Constant | Value | Description |
|---|---|---|
| `SAMPLE_SIZE` | 20,000 | Max rows to sample per table |
| `PRE_FILTER_PERCENTAGE` | 50 | % of rows to pass through ORA_HASH pre-filter |
| `SCHEMA` | `"SAPP70"` | Oracle schema name |
| `SOURCE_NAME` | `"p70"` | Source identifier in output |

---

### 3.4 `datasets/automate_syncs.py` -- Automated Sync Creation

**Path:** `transforms-python/src/myproject/datasets/automate_syncs.py`

**Purpose:** An **operational/setup transform** that programmatically creates new Foundry JDBC syncs (Magritte extracts) for each SAP table. This is typically run **once** to bootstrap the sync infrastructure, not as part of recurring builds.

**Transform Type:** `@transform` (Spark, Kubernetes No Executors profile)

**External Systems:** Uses a TPA (Third Party Application) source for authentication (`ri.magritte..source.96853095-ac12-4fa6-9425-df47a341ef1a`).

**Inputs:**

| Parameter | Dataset | Description |
|---|---|---|
| `p70_metadata` | `ri.foundry.main.dataset.7354d0e3-73e4-4bf7-94dd-f1aeba3a2bdd` (branch: `dev`) | Metadata from `sync_metadata.py` |

**Output:**

| Parameter | Dataset | Description |
|---|---|---|
| `out` | `/Global/global-data-source-sap-p70/logic/scratch/datasets/test/automate_syncs` | Sync creation results |

**Processing Steps:**
1. **Prepare table list** -- Builds sync specs from the `tables` list, formatting Oracle schema/table references and target dataset names.
2. **Check for collisions** -- Verifies no datasets with the planned names already exist in the target folder (`ri.compass.main.folder.827b04b3-...`).
3. **Create datasets** -- Uses `CatalogClient` to create empty datasets in Foundry.
4. **Load metadata** -- Reads the Oracle sampling queries from `p70_metadata`.
5. **Create syncs** -- Uses `MagritteCoordinatorClient` to set up JDBC extracts (SNAPSHOT type) using the pre-built Oracle queries.
6. **Write results** -- Outputs a DataFrame containing table names, dataset RIDs, and sync RIDs.

**Dependencies (Foundry Dev Tools):**
- `FoundryContext`, `JWTTokenProvider` / `OAuthTokenProvider`
- `CompassClient`, `CatalogClient`, `MagritteCoordinatorClient`

> **Note:** The JWT token field is empty in the code -- a personal token or TPA credentials must be supplied before execution.

---

### 3.5 `datasets/job_batches/config_class.py` -- Job Configuration Dataclass

**Path:** `transforms-python/src/myproject/datasets/job_batches/config_class.py`

**Purpose:** Defines the `SapAndSnowflakeChecksJobConfig` dataclass used to configure each SAP-to-Snowflake comparison job.

**Fields:**

| Field | Type | Default | Description |
|---|---|---|---|
| `table_name` | `str` | *(required)* | SAP table name (e.g., `"EKET"`) |
| `vt_rid` | `str` | *(required)* | Foundry Virtual Table RID for the Snowflake table |
| `sap_sample_rid` | `str` | *(required)* | Foundry dataset RID containing the JDBC-sampled SAP data |
| `primary_keys` | `List[str]` | `None` | Optional list of primary key columns |
| `output_folder_path` | `str` | `/Global/global-data-source-sap-p70/logic/scratch/datasets/comparisons` | Foundry path where comparison results are written |
| `cpu_cores` | `int` | `4` | CPU cores allocated to the lightweight transform |
| `memory_gb` | `int` | `30` | Memory (GB) allocated to the lightweight transform |
| `max_file_size` | `int` | `134,217,728` (~128 MB) | Max file size for Parquet output |
| `file_format_type` | `str` | `"parquet"` | Output file format |
| `cols_to_exclude` | `List` | `[]` | Columns to skip during comparison (e.g., columns missing in Snowflake) |

---

### 3.6 `datasets/job_batches/batches/batch_1.py` -- Batch 1 Configuration

**Path:** `transforms-python/src/myproject/datasets/job_batches/batches/batch_1.py`

**Purpose:** Defines the first batch of tables to compare. Each entry is a `SapAndSnowflakeChecksJobConfig` instance.

**Currently Configured Tables:**

| Table | VT RID | SAP Sample RID | Notes |
|---|---|---|---|
| `AFPO` | `ri.tables.main.table.f80f7d1c-...` | `ri.foundry.main.dataset.ae4d7954-...` | Production orders |
| `EKET` | `ri.tables.main.table.47119ebf-...` | `ri.foundry.main.dataset.f4da8970-...` | Schedule line data |
| `LIPS` | `ri.tables.main.table.071d0dcd-...` | `ri.foundry.main.dataset.267b5cc3-...` | Delivery items; excludes `J_1B_TAX_SITUATION` column |

---

### 3.7 `datasets/job_batches/merged_configs.py` -- Merged Configuration Registry

**Path:** `transforms-python/src/myproject/datasets/job_batches/merged_configs.py`

**Purpose:** Aggregates all batch configurations into a single `CONFIGURATIONS` list. This is the central registry consumed by the comparison engine and the results aggregator.

**Key Behavior:**
- Imports from each batch file (currently only `batch_1`).
- Unpacks all batch lists into `CONFIGURATIONS`.

```python
CONFIGURATIONS: list[SapAndSnowflakeChecksJobConfig] = [
    *batch_1.BATCH_1,
]
```

**To add a new batch:** Create a new file (e.g., `batch_2.py`) following the `batch_1.py` pattern, import it here, and spread it into `CONFIGURATIONS`.

---

### 3.8 `datasets/comparison/sync_sf_and_compare.py` -- SAP vs. Snowflake Comparison Engine

**Path:** `transforms-python/src/myproject/datasets/comparison/sync_sf_and_compare.py`

**Purpose:** The **core comparison engine**. For each configured table, this module generates a lightweight transform that:
1. Connects to Snowflake via `fast_snowflake_ingestion` (FSI) and Snowpark.
2. Reads the SAP sample data from Foundry.
3. Performs schema, row count, and sample-level comparisons.
4. Writes structured JSON results and any mismatched rows to an output dataset.

**Transform Type:** `@lightweight` (per-table, dynamically generated)

**Resources per transform:** Configurable via `SapAndSnowflakeChecksJobConfig` (default: 4 cores, 30 GB)

**Inputs (per table):**

| Parameter | Source | Description |
|---|---|---|
| `virtual_table` | `TableInput(config.vt_rid)` | Snowflake table via Foundry Tables API |
| `sample` | `Input(config.sap_sample_rid)` | JDBC-sampled SAP data |
| `p70_metadata` | `ri.foundry.main.dataset.7354d0e3-...` | DD03L-derived metadata (schema, PKs) |

**Output (per table):**
- Path: `{config.output_folder_path}/{config.table_name}` (e.g., `/Global/.../comparisons/EKET`)
- Contains: `_results.json` (structured test results) + Parquet data of unmatched rows (if any)

**Comparison Logic Detail:**

#### Schema Comparison
- Loads SAP schema from the `p70_metadata` dataset's `schema_json` column.
- Maps each SAP ABAP data type to expected Snowflake type(s) using `SAP_TO_SNOWFLAKE_TYPE_MAP`.
- Identifies:
  - Columns only in SAP
  - Columns only in Snowflake (excluding HVR replication columns: `HVR_IS_DELETED`, `HVR_CHANGE_TIME`, `HVR_CHANGE_SEQ`)
  - Type mismatches

**Result Values:** `PASS` (columns match + types compatible), `WARN` (columns match but types differ), `FAIL` (columns missing)

#### Row Count Comparison
- Extracts `SAP_TOTAL_ROW_COUNT` from the SAP sample (embedded via the Oracle subquery).
- Counts total rows in Snowflake via `source_sdf.count()`.
- Computes absolute difference.

**Result Values:** `PASS` (counts equal), `FAIL` (counts differ), `UNKNOWN` (SAP count unavailable)

#### Sample Row Comparison
- Performs a **left anti-join** of SAP sample rows against Snowflake data in Snowpark.
- Excludes system columns (`H`, `SAP_TOTAL_ROW_COUNT`) and config-specified columns.
- If unexpected columns are missing from Snowflake, the comparison is skipped with an error note.

**Key Constants:**

| Constant | Value | Description |
|---|---|---|
| `SNOWFLAKE_COLUMNS_TO_EXCLUDE` | `{HVR_IS_DELETED, HVR_CHANGE_TIME, HVR_CHANGE_SEQ}` | HVR replication metadata columns |
| `SAMPLE_COLUMNS_TO_EXCLUDE` | `{H, SAP_TOTAL_ROW_COUNT}` | Foundry/Oracle helper columns |

**SAP-to-Snowflake Type Mapping (`SAP_TO_SNOWFLAKE_TYPE_MAP`):**

The module includes a comprehensive mapping of ~50 SAP ABAP data types to their compatible Snowflake/Snowpark types. Key mappings include:

| SAP Type Category | SAP Types | Expected Snowflake Types |
|---|---|---|
| Character/String | CHAR, CLNT, CUKY, LANG, NUMC, UNIT, etc. | StringType |
| Integer | INT1, INT2, INT4, INT8 | LongType, DecimalType, IntegerType |
| Decimal/Packed | DEC, CURR, QUAN | DecimalType, LongType |
| Floating Point | FLTP | DoubleType, FloatType |
| Date/Time | DATS, TIMS | StringType, DateType, TimeType |
| Binary/Raw | RAW, LRAW | BinaryType, StringType |

**Helper Functions:**

| Function | Description |
|---|---|
| `_init_test_results(table_name)` | Initializes the result dictionary structure |
| `_evaluate_row_count_result(test_results)` | Evaluates PASS/FAIL for row counts |
| `_load_sap_schema(p70_metadata, table_name)` | Loads and parses schema JSON from metadata |
| `_populate_schema_results(test_results, schema_result)` | Maps schema comparison output into result dict |
| `_log_comparison_results(test_results, schema_result)` | Logs comparison details for debugging |
| `_write_test_results_to_output(fsi_instance, test_results)` | Writes `_results.json` via FSI file upload |
| `check_sap_to_snowflake_compatibility(sap_type, snow_type)` | Checks type compatibility |
| `compare_schemas_with_sap_metadata(sap_schema, snowflake_sdf, columns)` | Full schema comparison |

**Dynamic Transform Generation:**
- `generate_sync_transforms(config)` -- Creates a list of transforms for one table config.
- `run_batches(configs)` -- Iterates over all configs and collects all generated transforms.
- The module-level `transforms = run_batches(CONFIGURATIONS)` triggers generation at import time, which is how Foundry discovers them via `discover_transforms`.

---

### 3.9 `datasets/combine_comparison_results.py` -- Results Aggregator

**Path:** `transforms-python/src/myproject/datasets/combine_comparison_results.py`

**Purpose:** A dynamically generated lightweight transform that reads the `_results.json` file from each per-table comparison output and combines them into a single, flat Polars DataFrame.

**Transform Type:** `@transform.using` (Lightweight)

**Inputs:** Dynamically generated from `CONFIGURATIONS` -- one input per table config, reading from `{config.output_folder_path}/{config.table_name}`.

**Output:**

| Parameter | Dataset | Description |
|---|---|---|
| `output` | `/Global/global-data-source-sap-p70/logic/scratch/datasets/comparison_results_combined` | Combined tabular results |

**Output Schema:**

| Column | Type | Description |
|---|---|---|
| `table_name` | String | SAP table name |
| `sample_num_rows_matched` | Int | Rows from SAP sample found in Snowflake |
| `sample_num_rows_not_matched` | Int | Rows from SAP sample NOT found in Snowflake |
| `sample_num_missing_cols` | String (JSON) | Columns missing from Snowflake |
| `skipped_sap_columns` | String (JSON) | Columns explicitly excluded from comparison |
| `schema_result` | String | PASS / WARN / FAIL |
| `schema_columns_match` | Boolean | Whether all SAP columns exist in Snowflake |
| `schema_all_types_compatible` | Boolean | Whether all types are compatible |
| `schema_only_in_sap` | String (JSON) | Columns only in SAP |
| `schema_only_in_snowflake` | String (JSON) | Columns only in Snowflake |
| `schema_mismatched_types` | String (JSON) | Columns with type mismatches |
| `total_row_count_result` | String | PASS / FAIL / UNKNOWN |
| `total_row_count_sap` | Int | Total SAP row count |
| `total_row_count_snowflake` | Int | Total Snowflake row count |
| `total_row_count_difference` | Int | Absolute difference |
| `full_results_json` | String (JSON) | Complete raw results for reference |

**Processing Steps:**
1. For each input dataset, lists files and finds `_results.json`.
2. Parses the JSON and flattens nested structures into tabular columns.
3. Concatenates all results into a single Polars DataFrame.
4. Writes the combined results to the output dataset.

---

## 4. Data Files (JSON)

### `datasets/comparison/eket.json`

**Purpose:** Example/reference output of a completed comparison for the **EKET** table (Schedule Line Agreement).

**Key Results:**
- **Schema:** PASS -- All 68 columns matched with compatible types.
- **Sample Comparison:** 20,000 rows matched, 0 not matched.
- **Row Count:** FAIL -- SAP: 2,114,313 vs. Snowflake: 2,131,830 (difference: 17,517 rows).

### `datasets/comparison/lips.json`

**Purpose:** Example/reference output for the **LIPS** table (Delivery Item Data).

**Key Results:**
- **Schema:** PASS -- All compared columns matched (excluding `J_1B_TAX_SITUATION`).
- **Sample Comparison:** 243 matched, 19,757 not matched out of 20,000.
- **Row Count:** FAIL -- SAP: 33,482,608 vs. Snowflake: 34,259,607 (difference: 776,999 rows).

---

## 5. How to Add a New Table for Comparison

Follow these steps to add a new SAP table to the automated comparison pipeline:

### Step 1: Ensure the Virtual Table exists

Verify the Snowflake-backed Virtual Table RID is available. Check `available_vts.py` or look up the table in Foundry.

### Step 2: Ensure the SAP sample dataset exists

The SAP sample must be synced via JDBC first. Either:
- Add the table to `tables_to_sync.py` and run `automate_syncs.py`, **or**
- Manually create a Magritte JDBC sync using the Oracle query from `sync_metadata.py`.

### Step 3: Create a config entry

Add a `SapAndSnowflakeChecksJobConfig` to an existing batch file or create a new one:

```python
SapAndSnowflakeChecksJobConfig(
    table_name="YOUR_TABLE",
    vt_rid="ri.tables.main.table.XXXXXXXX-...",
    sap_sample_rid="ri.foundry.main.dataset.XXXXXXXX-...",
    cols_to_exclude=["OPTIONAL_COL_TO_SKIP"],  # if needed
)
```

### Step 4: Register in merged_configs.py

If using a new batch file, import and spread it into `CONFIGURATIONS`.

### Step 5: Commit and build

Push the changes. The pipeline will automatically discover the new transforms and produce comparison outputs.

---

|---|---|---|
